JOHN GARBER IS HOST AT
A REMARKABLE BREAKFAST
Millionaires Talk of the
White Mans Burden.
Huntingdon's Advice and Motto Is
to Grab Everything
in Sight
JOHN OARBER is the hero of local ,
clubdom. He, who is esteemed to
be the leader of the California bar
and one whose light is not dimmed
even in the brilliance of any
American legal constellation, has won
a triumph second to none in his history.
Hp has drawn around a breakfast
board the brightest and ablest minds
of the State and some of the nation. :
Under the inspiration of jrood fellow- i
ship and wine sparkling spirits have
flowed in wit and argument and men,
representing the highest achievements j
in mind and matter, money and brain,
have discussed with enthusiasm what
we ought to do or what we ought not
to do with the great problem of ex- |
MOST IsTOTJLBXjE BREAKFAST OF THIS SEASOU.
pansion, particularly as it is presented
in the Philippines.
The breakfast began with cocktails
and ended with a divided sentiment
that tho white men of the West should
take up the burden of the brown men
of the East. Strange as it may seem
decided efforts have been madp to keep
the incident of the Garber breakfast a
F^-ret. Why this should havp been
done no one seems to know except it
be that such master spirits as John
Garber drew t n his breakfast board
wish modestly to conceal their thoughts
upon great themes from the inspection
anl discussion />f the vulgar public.
The breakfast was given a week ago
last Saturday at the Pacific Union
Club. Among those who wÂ»re present
wfre D. O. Mills. C. P. Huntlngton.
Professor David Starr Jordan, Profr-H
--por Martin Kellogg, Whitelaw Reid,
Rabbi Jacob Voorsanger, Professor Jo- .
s'ph Le Conte, John Garber, Rev.
Thomas R. Bacon. George Crocker,
Supreme Justice Ralph C. Harrison, W.

ace G. Platt. Charles Webb Howard
and Professor George H. Howison. The
grouping of such men as these at a sin
SAN FRANCISCO, TUESDAY, APRIL 18, 1899.
gle breakfast board was John Garner's
triumph. Men prominent in the field of
finance, letters, law, religion, philos
ophy, politics, education and in that
peculiar science which C. P. Hunting
ton arrogates to himself, were at that
I'inrd. . As might have been expected
the result was of exceptional interest.
At the conclusion of the breakfast,
which began at noon and was not end
i ed until 5 o'clock in the afternoon, the
thoughts of those present turned to a
discussion of timely topics, particularly
j of that which is of primary moment to
the people of the West â the possession
of the Philippines. The aggregated
wealth represented at that board might
buy the Philippines, but that was not
the point. Millionaires do not discuss
seriously at breakfast questions of bar
ter and sale.
Professor Le Conte opened the dis
cussion. In eloquent seriousness the
aged scholar argued against the theory
of America's expansion. America's
destiny, the speaker said, is not in the
direction of the Philippine Islands.
There is something more in American
ideals than commerce and commercial
success, and the honored professor of
Berkeley hoped that American states
men will realize this before it is too
late to return to firmer ground than
that upon which they now stand. The
polished eloquence of Professor Le
Conte may have been the inspiration
for the enthusiasm of the subsequent
discussion which reached a most un
expected climax in a personal debate
between Professor Howison and Rabbi
Voorsanger.
Professor Jordan followed Professor
Le Conte In the argument, and with the
success with which he attacks any sub
ject, acquitted himself well to the satis
faction of his hearers. Professor Jor
dan argued against expansion, as he is
sincerely of the opinion that the white
man has troubles of his own, without
taking unto himself those of the brown
men.
There was a murmur of gratified sur
prise wh"n C. P. Huntington gracicrus
ly consented to discuss the theme of the
afternoon. Everybody, present raised
his glass and drank in acknowledg
ment of the magnate's condescension.
<"". P. Huntington was in a pleasant
mood. More than that, ho was candid.
He made an announcement that is of
rational interest. He assured his hear-
ers that he never does anything for his
health, which is surprising in one of his
age, and that when he makes am ove he
knows what he is after. It was not
generally noticed that at this remark
George Crocker again drank the old
man's health.
Huntington annnunceil himself em
phatically in favor of expansion and on
the American possession of the Philip
pines. If we behave ourselves, Hunt
ington says that in two or three years
he may be building railroads in the
Philippines. That there might be no
mistake in reference to his meaning,
and that his bluntness might not be
misconstrued, the old railroad magnate
spoke from notes.
"Gentlemen," he Bald, "I believe that
expansion is a good thing for the com
mercial interests of this country. I
believe in snatching everything in
sight."
George Crocker again raised his glass
to his lips and Huntington #ontinued:
"That has been my mntto for a num
ber of years ;tnd I find that it pays far
better than conservatism. Expansion
would help California and especially !
Ran Francisco. I might mention that
in two or three years; if everything
turns out all right, th^ Southern Pacific
Company will be building railroads in
the Philippines. You are aware, per
haps, that we never do anything for
our health.
"They can do all the talking they
want to and caricature the ra*v work
of your Uncle Collis as they please, but
when the old man makes a move he un
derstands what he is after."
These words alone would make John
Garher's breakfast famous. Hunting
ton resumed his seat and received the
tribute of marked applause. The gen
tlemen at the board were becoming en
thusiastic. Even John Garber forgot
his staid dignity as host and applauded
with frequent interruptions of ap
proval.
D. O. Mills followed Huntington in
an advocacy of the same policy which
had been championed by the president
of the Southern Pacific Company. Mr.
Mills, under the inspiration of the
moment and the contagion of sympa
thetic spirits, declared that we will
neglect our manifest destiny if we do
not keep the Philippines and go further
to take a slice of China.
"While other nations," he said, "are
making themselves numerous it would
be a good thing for us to take a slice
of China while we are at it."
D. 0, Mills Urges Us to
Take a Slice of China*
Dr* Voorsanger, Professor How
ison and Joseph Le Conte in
Debate.
This constant suggestion to grab
everything in sight seemed to have a
depressing effect upon George Crocker.
As might have been expected, Pro
fessor George Howison agreed in opin
ion with D. O. Mills. It will be remem
bered, perhaps, that D. O. Mills Is re
sponsible for the chair of philosophy
which Professor Howison so creditably
fills at Berkeley.
When Professor Howison arose to
speak there fell upon the feasters a si
lence due to the abilities and opinions
of the distinguished scholar. Professor
Hnwison sees in the intervention of the
United States in the Philippines an act
of God. It is our God-given duty, he
says, to take up the burden that the
success of our arms has thrust upon us.
"The intervention of the United
States in the Philippines," he said, "is
an act of the providence of God. We
have received a God-given trust. It is
our duty to take up the white man's
burden and to show the effete mon
archies of Europe that when it comes
to enlightening the heathen and up
lifting humanity the United States are
in the van. This is the reason and
the justification for the interference of
the United States in the Philippines."
Oh most occasions the Rev. Dr. Jacob
Voorsanger is wrapped in a mantel of
self-restraint. He seldom gives ex
pression to emphatic opinions if he has
any, but it seems that the reverend
gentleman entertains, on his own ac
count, some very pronounced ideas on
the white man's burden. They are not
in accord with those of Professor Ho,w
ison, al.'.ough in matters philosophic
the two gentlemen are more frequently
in accord than in disagreement.
When Professor Howison saw in the
conquest of the Philippines the direct
act of God the Rev. Dr. Voorsanger
made caustic reply. He is unable to
see God anywhere in the affair ami
more than that he does not believe that
the vision of Professor Howison is any
keener than his own. He cannot bring
himself to believe that God takes any
particular pleasure in seeing Undo
fam's soldiers drill Filipino warriors
full of holes. Money and markets are
the bait, the reverend gentleman in
sists.
"I want to disagree," he said at the
breakfast, "with the men of finance. It
seems to me that it is the very height
of fnlly for us to pretend that the in
terference of this Government in the
Philippines will do the natives any
good. What we are after there and
what we are seeking is new territory
for business purposes, for rTew markets
and new trade. For heaven's sake, let
us not delude ourselves that when we
go down to the Philippines with our
men-of-war and Gatling guns and drill
the natives full of holes we are follow
ing the desire of God. We are there
for money and markets, and for no
other reason in the world, and Professor
Howison knows it."
The closing remark cf the reverend
gentleman provoked a most heated and
somewhat personal discussion between
Professor Howison and Dr. Voorsanger.
There was a series of explanations, a
renewal of all sorts of good fellowship
and an agreement that the white man's
burden would not again become the
personal concern of those present. In
the argument Mr. Foote remarked that
if any of the gentlemen who seemed to
be bo personally interested found the
white man's burden too heavy on their
side of the table they could transfer it
and its obligations to him.
Whitelaw Reid then made a
few remarks, but discreetly dealt
in generalities that were â¢ not
glittering. His experience as a
diplomat abroad probably made him
discreet. Then there was a call for
George Crocker, but he had gone. It
is not safe to leave Uncle Collis in one
place and the nephew in the other, and
George Crocker is burdened with
enough gold bricks not to take Uncle
Collis at his word. William F. Herrin,

THE BRITISH
An Advance Upon the Camp
at Talpohu Checked by
Gascoigne s Force.
QUICKLY DRIVEN BACK
Imperial Troops Retire From the
Fierce Fire and Burn Villages
During the Retreat.
Special Cable to The Call and the New York
HeraM. Copyrighted, Is:'*, by James Gor
don Bennett.
HONGKONG, April 17.â
There was no fighting
yesterday. To-day at
noon thousands of Chinese ad
vanced toward the British camp
at Talpohu and fired several
rounds of artillery at ineffective
range.
Major General Gascoigne,
whose" force is less than 300, or
dered his Asiatic artillery to en
gage the enemy, who occupied
the surrounding hills. Shrapnel
drove the Chinese out and the
Indian infantry advanced, the
Chinese retiring several miles,
burning villages.
The Chinese losses are un
known, as they are carrying
away their dead and wounded.
The British had no loss.
It is proposed to advance the
British position to-morrow.
With the exception of his staff,
General Gascoigne is utilizing
only the Indian troops.
Hongkong is guarded by vol
unteers and the Welsh Fusileers.
FIERCE REBELS
UNFURL THEIR
GREEN BANNER
Chinese Turkestan at the
Mercy of the Savage
Salah Mussulmans.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
VICTORIA, B. C, April 17.â Late
Chinese papers say news comes from
Lanchou. capital of Kansu province,
that the Mohammedan communities to
the north-west of the cityâ known as
the Salah Mussulmans, the most sav
age and bigoted of that faith in the
Chinese dominionsâ have rebelled. The
green sacred banner of the prophet has
been unfurled by their Akhoon or chief
Mullah, and the word passed to all the
Mohammedan townships in Kansu.
Shensi and Chinese Turkestan to wage
war to the knife upon the Buddhist un
believers.
A telegram from Governor Jao of
Chinese Turkestan, whose capital is
Urnmsti. has also been received at Pe
king, reporting considerable unrest
among the Tunganis (Chinese Moham
medans) and Tarantchis in Kuldja and
Turkis in Kashgaria.
A later dispatch from Viceroy Tao of
Kansu has been received at Peking, re
porting the massacre of over thirty
Chinese Buddhists by rebel Salah Mo
hammedans. A general conflagration is
feared with the return of warm weather
in April or May, when communication
becomes easier, unless a strong army
is assembled in Kansu.
The Viceroy complained of the with
drawal to Peking of Generals Tung
Fuchsiang and Chang Chun with their
best troops, whose sanguinary battles
of the past twenty years against the
Mohammedans have made them greatly
feared by the latter, not only in Kansu,
but also in Chinese Turkestan, and that
in consequence the whole northwest is
practically defenseless and at the
mercy of the Mohammedans, if tnej
should unite together and rebel.

PIONEER HAGKMAN McGEORGE
PASSES AWAY AT SANTA ROSA
SAXTA ROSA. April 17.â Robert Mc-
George, the pioneer hackman of this
city and the oldest hack driver in
California, died this morninfr. He
J v. iks. For more
forty years "Mack," a3 he was fa
miliarly known to the traveling public,
has driven a public carriage in this city.
;gh at the time of Ills death he had
passed the 78th milestone, until the be
ginning of his recent illness he drove
with all his well-known skill and climbed
on and off the box like a man of 40. Dur
ing his long handling of the reins he had
driven almost every prominent visitor
about the city, and there is little doubt
that he enjoyed a wider acquaintance
than any other man in his profession In
this State.
About a year ago McGeorge was at
tacked by a cancer. He laid aside the
reins for a few months and underwent a
course of treatment that was apparently
successful. Returning again to his old
work, he kept it up with all his old-time
force until a few weeks ago, when a huge
carbuncle appeared upon the back of his
neck. Owing to the condition of his blood
it caused his death.
McGeorge was a native of England.
He leaves a widow.

A DAUGEROTJS BLAZE.
Promptness of the Fire Department
Averts a Big Fire.
But for the prompt arrival of the chem
ical engine the branch factory of the Ber
nard Mattress Company at 651 Mission
street, would have been destroyed by fire
yesterday afternoon and the frame build
ings in the vicinity might also have suf
fered. The contents of the "shoddy" room
on the top floor of the establishment were
ignited by sparks from the "picking ma
chine, and the inflammable stuff burned
briskly. An alarm was turned In from
the auxiliary box, and the employes made
every effort to prevent the flames from
spreading. Had they not worked ener
getically a large quantity of hay stored
near the "shoddy*" would have caught
fire. The firemen soon had the blaze un
der control and the danger was averted.
The "picking" machines were damaged
to the extent of $500. The damage to the
building was nominal^ "^ ' â -

THE SAX- FR AX CISCO CALL, TUESDAY, APRIL 18, 1899.
PHILOSOPHY OF
LIFE DISCUSSED
Universal Brotherhood's
Work and Aims.
MAN'S DUTY TOWARD MAN
HELPING HAND TO BRIGHTEN
HUMAN EXISTENCE.
Views Expressed by Noted Theoso
phists Before a Vast Gathering
in the Point Loma
Amphitheater.
BY MRS. H. H. S3MLRS.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SAN DIEGO, April 17.â Fully 2000 peo
ple gathered in the amphitheater on the
site of the School for the Revival of <...Â«
Lost Mysteries of Antiquity on Point
Loma on Sunday afternoon to hear ad
dresses by representatives present from
all over the world on -"The Philosophy of
Life." The presence of this large audi
ence, considering the rather inconvenient
means of reaching Point Loma from San
Diego, indicated a strong interest among
the people of San Diego and vicinity in
the work and ideas of the Universal
Brotherhood.
As soon as the meeting was formally
declared open by the permanent chair
man of the congress, Mr. E. Aug. Ner
shelmer, chairman of the- Universal
Brotherhood, read a list of appointees to
the international council, which was soon
to extend throughout the globe, embrac
ing all of the principal nations and many
minor countries. The names of the mem
bers of the national council were then
read. This body is composed of represen
tative Americans In all parts of the |
Union. |
P. M. Pierce, secretary general of the |
Universal Brotherhood, read many greet
ings to the congress by letter, telegram
and cable, which had been received since j
the previous session. These come from :
lodges in Sweden, Holland, Germany,
England, New Zealand, Canada, India
and other countries and from cities In
many States.
The orchestra rendered a splendid mu
sical selection, after which "The Philoso
phy of Life" was taken up by a number >
of speakers. . The scope of the subject ad
mitted of discussion from many points of j
view. It was accordingly treated, but at
the same time a broad unanimity be
tween the speakers was apparent. The.
basic idea of all was the necessity of rec- ;
ognizing brotherhood as a fundamental ,
fact â¢â ! human existence, and the making
of it the rule of action in the life of man
kind. All the great .philosophers were
declared to have been raised up on this
foundation, different systems of thought i
having been evolved in all ages and peo- j
ples as were appropriate to the needs
of the races and the time.
Several speakers maintained the advisa- ;
bility of giving less attention to systems
of philosophy and depending more upon
sound common-sense, regarding such as
the coping stone and purpose of every i
school of philosophy. Differences of opin- j
ion were regarded as unimportant ana ad '
the natural outcome of different condi- '
tions and development of mind, but in !
every case brotherhood as a fact and the
essential wisdom of making it the guid
ing rule were insisted upon.
Mrs. Tingley paid in substance that the great
need of lite now was to recognize and realize
conceptions. The weakness into which man had
drifted left him now without the courage and
strength to battle with the force of adverse |
conditions. In accord with his higher feelings
and aspirations. lie felt that he had not the j
time to work except on the prevailing lines, and j
these led to pain. Buffering and helplessness.
She spoke of the people of Cuba, of their pow- !
erlessness unaided and alone to avail them- :
selves of the opportunity and the new life open- '
Ing to them; what this could be brought to
â with some compassionate aid given with the
touch of common human kindness. She re
ferred to all this as but the fuller expression
of what was prevailing In loss accentuated form
everywhere. When humanity, through the en
couragement of brotherly help and sympathy
and the higher education and deeper under
standing such would bring to pass, came to
know and fill the soul, then would men and
women arise with the power to lead human life ]
Into its rightful heritage. This was the aim |
and purpose of the Universal Brotherhood, and |
already its work was producing results and was
witnessed by the rate at which the organization
was growing, by the presence of that large
assemblage, containing so many who In devo
tion and love to this cause and to promote it
had come from the furthermost parts of the
globe.
The Wagner recital was given Sunday night
by Mrs. â¢ Alice Leighton Cleather and Basil
Crump of London, a noted member of the Lon
don Wagner Society and of the Universal
Brotherhood. The amphitheater was tilled to
overflowing. Splendid stereoptlcon views were
used to bring out and accentuate the vital
teachings of the great philosopher and the es
sential characters of hip most important
dramas. Mrs. Cleather and Mr. Crump when
speaking were practically in the dark, this
being necessary in the use of the stereopttcon,
there being Just enough light to give' a glimpse
of Greek life depicted on the scenery behind,
which was in place for the presentation of the
great play, "Eumenldes," to come a little later.
The darkness added to the lmpressiveness of
the occasion and aided the understanding to
ward better grasping .the real meaning of the
Interpretation given by the lecturers.
Mrs. Cleather and Mr. I'rumn alternately
spoke. in the interval retiring behind the dimly
visible palms, whence the imposing soul-stir
ring music, rendered by them came a3 an in
spiring power to the audience. No one present
could have failed to recognize somewhat of the
grand function of the drama and music in
union will perform for the individual and for
the state when it is rightly understood and
utilized, and to see that such was the hope and
aim to which Wagner devoted the genius and
best energies of hlB life.
Every preparation has been made for the first
presentation of "Eumenides," to occur on
Monday evening. To its production the Wagner
recital forms a fitting prelude. It will demon
strate in fuller measure than the Wagner re
cital the truth of the Ideal and profound con
ceptions to which â¢ Wagner labored to give
birth. .
A special congressional session of Universal
Brotherhood will convene immediately after the
regular one now proceeding will have expired
by limitation. The special session will extend
through threo days to Sunday, April 23. It. is
called because many matters of Importance
have not been reached and additional ones have
arisen which need prompt attention and con
sideration. More time for this is required than
Is left at the disposal of the regular session.
HELD UP AT THE
POINT OF A PISTOL
Advertising Manager Joselyn of the
Emporium Robbed by Footpads
of Money and Trinkets.
W. E. Joslyn. advertising manager of
the Emporium, was waylaid by two foot
pads shortly after midnight Saturday and
at the point of a pistol compelled to surÂ»
render the sum of $62 50 and a number of
trinkets. He reported the robbery to thÂ»
police last night and Detective Silvey Is
searching for the daring criminals.
Mr. Joslyn stated that he was on his
way to his residence at 82S Powell street,
and while climbing the hill between PlnÂ»
and California streets was startled by
two men Jumping out of a dark door
way and ordering him to give up his val
uables. â  â 
The attack was so sudden that Mr.
Joslyn hud no chance to resist. With a
pistol within an inch of his nose the vic
tim handed over a small sum, but one of
the robbers heard the click of his purse
clasp and compelled him to give it up.
The robbers were scared by some peo
ple passing on California street, and they
ordered Mr. Joslyn to climb the hill. Only
too glad to escape, the latter moved rap
idly up the street, and when he turned
around to see if they were still there he
was warned by one of them to get out of
sight or he would put a bullet through
Mr. Joslyn described the men as being
tall and short. The tall man wore a red
handkerchief on the lower part of his
face and the short man a white one. The
former was attired In a dark suit and
wore a white fedora hat. The short man
had a long overcoat that reached to his
ankles. Mr. Joslyn thinks he could, identi
fy, them if he ever saw,. them agalp,
WOODLAND'S CITY TRUSTEES
ELECT A. M. BRITT MAYOR
WOODLAND, April 17.â The
City Trustees met to-day and
canvassed the election re
turns. There was no change
from the result heretofore
published. Upon the announcement of
the result Messrs. Bandy and Hiddle
son, the retiring members, stepped
down and out and W. P. Craig and T.
B. Gibson were sworn in. The board
reorganized by unanimously electing
A. M. Britt Mayor.
The new Mayor is a popular young
man of good business ability and sterl
ing Integrity. He is influential in the
order of Native Sons. His many friends
predict that his administration will be
energetic, efficient and economical.
STANFORD'S NEW
REGISTER ISSUED
Important Changes Put
Into Effect.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
STANFORD UNIVERSITY, April 17.
The first copies of the university reg
ister for 1898-99 made their appearance
to-day. The book, in arrangement and
general appearance, corresponds closely
to the catalogues of previous years. It
will be ready for general distribution
in a few days.
The most important change noted In
the new register is the one regarding
entrance requirements. It is in line
â with the policy being pursued through
out the country in requiring students
to have a more thorough preparation
before entering college than they have
had in the past. Beginning in Septem
ber, 1900, thirteen credits will be re
quired for admission in full standing,
Instead of twelve as at present. With
the commencement of the academic
year 1901, â¢ fifteen credits will be re
quired for regular standing. After 1901
partial standing will require twelve
instead of. ten credits. The change has
been made in the interest of intercol
legiate comity, to facilitate the transfer
of students from one institution to an
other and to bring the requirements of
Stanford into as exact correspondence
as possible with those of the leading
Institutions of the country.
This increase in the entrance credits
required does not mean that prepara
tory students must take up more
studies. It will allow them to pursue
mure advanced work along certain
lines, lor arrangements have been made
in Greek, German and French whereby
extra credits can be obtained.
One effect of the new requirements
upon the high schools will be this: Be
ginnlng with IWI it will be impossible
for any high school to prepare for ad
mlssion in full standing on a three
years' course, it will be possible, how
ever, for graduates of the three years'
course to enter the university in partial
Btandlng. The result of the change will
lie to hasten the transition in the high
Bchoors from the three to the four
years' course without actually compel
ling it.
Under admission on recommendation
the tendency to require something more
than the statement oJ the principal la
ting more marked. It will be
isary to present some satisfactory
evidence of the student's work here
after. In English literature the experi
m< at of giving an oral examination will
be tried. In history the note-book re
quirement goes into effect this year. In
physiology, botany and zoology recom
mendations will be received, provided
they are accompanied, by satisfactory
note books. In French and German,
after 1899, exercise books in French
composition must be submitted.
Among the new courses offered this
year are two In Russian, by Professor
Bablne. Mathematics, for the first time,
is arranged under two separate titles
pure mathematics and applied mathe
matics.
The number of students for the pres
ent year Is larger than for any previous
year of the university except 1897-Os.
There Is a small decrease in the num
ber of graduate students. A greater
falling off Is noticed in the special stu
dents, due to the severe requirements
put into effect this year in raising the
age limit and establishing the require
ment of being able to do advanced work
in some line.
In the faculty list Professor Bran
ner's name appears next In order to
President Jordan's, with his new title
of vice president. Professors Spencer
and Cubberly have been advanced
from assistant to associate professors.
Professors Copeland and Newson have
1 ti added to the list of assistant pro
fessors and Professor Whittier has been
promoted from an Instructor to an as
sistant professor.
Tho now Instructors are Miss Maud
March, Jefferson Elmore, J. H. Howard,
Robert E. Swain, O. L. Lincoln and
â¢Jackson E. Reynolds.
The non-resident lecturers for the
year include Professor Hunt of the
University of the Pacific, Judge Llnd
ley of Ran Francisco and Rabbi Voor
eanger of San Francisco.
The library now contains 42,000 vol
umes and 18,000 pamphlets. The faculty
â consisting of professors, associate and
assistant professors, instructors and
lecturersânumbers 107. Of the 1153 stu
dents 690 are men and 463 are women.

IN ON THE COPTIC
BOTH ARE MAKING A TOUR FOR
HEALTH AND PLEASURE.
The Pacific Mail Steamship Company's
Coptic arrived last night, and after dis
charging 100 Chinese passengers en route
to Mexico at Angel Island anchored off
her dock. A number of passengers who
were anxious to get ashore were taken
off in a tug. Prominent on her passenger
list were Prince Kannote of Japan, Presi
dent of the House of Peers; Prince Llch
reowski, formerly connected with the
German legation at Athens; Stanley Gib
bon, the well-known stamp collector; B.
B. Barney of Los Angeles, A. H. Pirle,
head of the educational system in India,
and Lieutenant Nickel of the Montana
troops. .
Prince Kannole is making a tour of the
world for pleasure and observation. He
does not think that all the talk of trouble
In China amounts to anything, but bays
that If China is to be partitioned by the
powers Japan will certainly want a slice
of it. He brings the news that the Japa
nese Government has consented to subsi
dize the two Japanese steamship compa
nies now plying vessels between this
country and the Orient. This means that
six new vessels. will be put on, three to
ply between Japan and San Francisco and
three running to Seattle or Victoria.
Prince Kannoie is not a Prince of the
blood, but worked himself up to his pres
ent position. He is bright and intelligent
and speaks English fluently. â¢
Prince Lichreowski is making a trip
around the world for his health. He Bays
he knows nothing of the Samoan difficul
ties save what he has read in the papers,
and "Hoes not look upon the trouble so
riouel}*
FUMES SWEEP
LONDON FLATS
Holocaust Is Narrowly
Averted.
Special Dispatch to Thf< Call.
LONDON, April 17.â The British
metropolis narrowly escaped a calamity
to-day which might have rivaled the
horrors witnessed at tho destruction by
fire recently of the Windsor Hotel in
New York city.
Hyde Park Court, Albertgate, one of
the finest and most fashionable blocks
of residential flats, overlooking the
famous Rotten Row, caught fire this
morning. The building, which is
eleven stories high, was built by the
notorious Jabez Spencer Balfo-ur, the
former member of Parliament, who was
responsible Cor the collapse of the
Liberator Building Association and
other companies, by which thousands o-f
people were ruined ;md who is now
undergoing a sentence of fourteen years
penal servitude as a result of his con
viction on charges of fraud. It prac
tically adjoins the house occupied by
Sir Herbert Naylor-Leyland, Bart.,
Liberal member of Parliament for the
Southport division of Lancanshire,
whose wife was Miss Annie Chamber
lain of Cleveland, O.
The fire-broke out in the lower part of
the building and spread up the elevator
shaft with great rapidity. The flames
Involved the upper stories before their
occupants were aware of the danger.
When the alarm was raised the af
frighted servants dropped from the top
windows to a veranda on the ninth
story. The defective arrangements of
the London fire brigade were again
demonstrated and even when the fire
escapes reached the scene they were
too short to reach the people in the up
per stories, wtio only escaped with the
greatest difficulty,, while the residents
of the lower stories streamed out of the
building, carrying what they could of
their belongings.
The flames were not under control
until 1 o'clock, when the upper part of
the structure was gutted.
It is considered lucky that the fire
occurred in daylight, as otherwise it
would have resulted in loss of life. All
the floors were supposed to be fire
proof.
The investigation into the cause of
the lire shows it to have been due to
the overheating of an electric wire.
Several of the occupants of the build
ing escaped in their nightclothes.
A whole army of tlremen, under Com
mander Wells, was engaged in subdu
ing the flames. During the conflagra
tion the Windows of the French Em
bassy, which overlooked tho scene,
werÂ»; crowded, and an Immense mn
eourso of people watched the fire from
the streets. The property destroyed in
cluded a quantity of jewelry.

EX-SENATOR VILAS HERE.
Cleveland's Postmaster General Tak
ing a Pleasure Trip.
Ex-Senator William F. Vilas of Madi
son, Wis., arrived in this city yesterday
in his private car and registered at the
Palace. With him are Mrs. Vilas, his
son-in-law and daughter, Mr. and Mrs.
B. F. Jones, and a cousin, Dr. C. H
Vilas.
The party has been at Pasadena for the
past two months, and after remaining
here for a week or ten days will leave
for home.
Senator Vilas has retired from politics
and now devotes himself fo the practice
of his profession, the law.
His visit to this coast is one entirely of
pleasure, the Senator taking a needed
rest after an unusually active career.

CLAIMS STAKED
UNDER CLAIMS
Peculiar Situation in the
Klondike.
PLACERS OVERLIE QUARTZ
PROSPECTORS FILE LOCATIONS
ON BEDROCK.
Owners of Creek Property Find That
Their Jurisdiction Extends but
a Few Feet Below the
Surface.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
T A COMA, April 17.â News comes
from Dawson that Dr. Willis E. Ever
ett, the well-known Tacoma mineralo
gist and assayer, has discovered that
bedrock at the bottom of the Klondike
placers is indented with seams contain
ing gold, which makes the bedrock
work $5000 and upward a ton. It is
reported Dr. Everett and a number of
other mining men have located the
bedrock of a number of the richest
placer mines as quartz claims. Placer
miners are greatly displeased at this
turn of affairs and claim Dr. Everett
has no right to obtain mines in thi^
manner. The matter has been referred
to the Gold Commissioner for a deci
sion.
Never in the history of placer min
ing in Alaska has any attention been
paid to bedrock. The gravel for a few
feet above it was always rich, and from
it most of the gold that has come from
Alaskan districts has been taken. The
miners have never gone any deeper,
nor did they think to have it assayed.
Sulphur Creek bedrock has proved the
richest around Dawson and many
quartz locations have been posted at
the bottom of the placer shafts along
the creek bed since Dr. Everett made
his discovery. As soon as the discovery
was announced there was a stampede
to Sulphur Creek, and now bedrock un
der placer claims is all located, from
claim 65 to 20 below. Dr. Everett is
reported as having said that the rock
will go as high as $10,000 in gold.
To prove that the gold is not the free
placer g"ld of the surface gravels,
which has been worked down into the
interstices of the bedrock, the sulphides
from ten square feet of ro<-k taken
from ten different places were care
fully washed six times. Then the sul

phides were subjected to the furnaces
and yielded $1500 to the ton which
means about $20 to the ton of bedrock
removed and simply washed The
question arisies: Does mineral-bearing
bedrock in place belong to the placer
claim above, or. is it subject to quartz
location by some one else?
Time will prove or disprove the
value of the discovery, but the ques
tion as to whether the new quartz lo
cators or placer men own the BearocK
may prove an unpleasant one to handle.
Hitherto placer men have been in the
habit of taking up a foot or more or
the bedrock for the free gold it con
tained. This, the quartz men say,
cannot be done, as the rock is in place
and is a schist containing gold of it
self. A simple way out of the dilemma
for the placer men is to locate their
own bedrock where they find it worth
working, as the cost of location is only
$5. and drifting in the gravel will an
swer as a representation of work. The
claims are perfectly safe from outsid
ers, for unless they really get to bed
rock they cannot locate it.

summer by announcing that he naa

gold. His gold button was misem
scopical in size and he made no claim
that his method of transmutation P"?
--sessed any commercial value. Last
fall Dr. Everett went to Dawson to re
main two- years in the interest of New
York and London men.
Death of a Chinese Hermit.
REDDING. April 17. -A Coroner's in
quest was held to-day over the body of Ah
Toy, a Chinese paralytic, who died in hig
hut near Shasta last night. Ah Toy had
lived in that one spot for upward of
twenty-five years. The hair on his head
had grown to more than the length or*
his body, and his finger nails had attain-'
ed great length. He died as he had lived,
alone and shunned by his fellow-country
men.

MAKING BRAINS.
Brain tissue is made of albumen act-
ed upon by delicate molecules of phos-
phate of potash.
These things are found in certain
foods supplied by Nature. Food ex-
perts have made use of this knowledge
to prepare Grape-Nuts, a food made up
of selected parts of the choicest Wheat
and Barley and prepared in a way as
ear as possible like nature handles
such food to turn it into grape-sugar
and to combine, in the body, the al-
bumen and phosphate of potash for re-
building the soft gray substance of the
bruin and nerve centers. Theory is
worthless if not sustained by substan-
tial proof.
Ten days use of Grape-Nuts will
bring to the user a certain sense of
increased mental and nervous power,
unmistakable and a welcome proof of
proper nourishment.

guished.
Special Dlsratch to The Coll.
CHICAGO, April 17.â The long-ex
pected ha? at last happened and writ
ers of funny paragraphers the world
over have been vindicated. The Chi
] ;iver took fire to-night and
burned fiercely for an hour.
Shortly before midnight a passenger
on a Northwestern suburban train,
while crossing the river at Kinzie street
just outside the Btatlon, carelessly
threw a burning match out of the win
after lighting a cigar. The river a
moment later was a sheet of flnme for
a distance of two or three blocks. The
clocks took lire, ;:s did the abutments
and wood work of the bridge.
In the bridge house were several bar
of oil used by the tender for his
signal lamps. They took fixe, exploded
and added their burning contents to the
already Haming mass below. A sewer
empties into the river at this point. A
large volume of gas poured out of thi:
and it, too, became ignited. All the
boats on the rivev and all engines In thÂ«
district were hurried to the scene to
save the docks, warehouses and eleva
tors in the vicinity. All the vessels in
the creek at that point were hurried un- j
der their own steam or by tugs Into the ;
south branch to avoid destruction.

into the river at the tannerii s som
tance above and held in a mass at the
point where the. fire was set by a slight

ACCIDENTALLY SHOT
WHILE HUNTING
SALINAS, April 17. â One of the saddest accidental shooting affairs
ever known in this section is reported from below Monterey. D. Castro,
a lad not fifteen years old, while hunting in the mountains near his
home, wasshot in the abdomen with a rifle ball, lay unconscious a long
time in the dense underbrush, and, when finally discovered, was car- 1
rled nearly a mile upon the back of his brother and then a long distance
further on horseback to the ranch house. He was bleeding and suf
fering tortures on the trip, and he finally died before medical aid could
be summoned.
Just how the accident occurred â will never be known. The unfor
tunate lad did not regain consciousness enough to speak and it is sup
posed there were no eye-witnesses to the affair. A. Castro, the brother
of the boy and the one who found him, said they were hunting, each
carried Winchesters. The country was very rugged. Tiny became
separated, one on either side nf a canyon. The elder brother heard two
reports ol a rifle and then a cry for help. He hastened in the direction
"f the call, found his brother lying on the ground, mortally wounded.
The boy had been making his way through heavy brush, dragging his
weapun behind. him, and it had been accidentally discharged.
Dccci Bed was the youngest Bon of Daniel ("astro, a n old citizen.

NEW YORK. April 17.- -A sperlnl cable

decided its fate, but its belligerent
strength.

what less sanguine of the success of the
conference, and now frankly admits that
if Russia is noi prepared to set the exam
ple of reducing her own armament she
cannot expect others to do so.

FILIPINOS
TO CONFER
AS TO PEACE
Continued from First Page.
General Lawton's retirement from the
Laguna de Bai district in Luzon, finds
in it nothing surprising or discourag
ing:.
"British generals have often had to
do the same thing," says the Times, "in
similar circumstances. A much graver
matter is the reported discontent of the
American tmops, but here also all of
the countries pursuing a foreign policy
have had the Paint- difficulties to con
tend with, and no doubt the I'nited
States will soon find it advisable to
imitate European measures and to en
list a regular force for the purpose of
h tiding the Philippines. Foreign pos
sessions cannot be held by volunteers."
The Daily Chronicle publishes this
morning an interview with Lieutenant
General Sir Andrew Clarke, agent gen
eral for Victoria and Tasmania, and
former Governor and commander-in
chief of the Straits Settlements, regard-
Ing American policy In the Philippine.-;.
Sir Andrew urges a thorough under
standing of the native character and a
government, so far as possible, by na
tive institutions. The Americans, in
his opinion, should first discover Agui
naldo's sources of supply and then turn
these Into agencies of trade for the set
tlement of the country. They should
show the natives that this would be a
better outlet for capital and they
would thus be depriving the enemy of
the sinews of war. After that, he con
tinues, they should buy out the re
ligious orders, with the sanction of the
Pope, and attach the secular clergy in
some way to the administration, secur
ing their powerful influence.
"Underlying everything," says Sir
Andrew, "must be the good will of the
Datives. Then, with carefully selected
administrators. the United States
would have nothing to fear from the
Filipinos."
The interview was supplemented by
a r-poj-t based upon the experience of
British administrators in the Straits
Settlements.

new colonies.

JUDGE ROBERTSON OF
TULARE TIRES OF LIFE
Broken in Health, a Popular Official
Attempts Suicide by Cutting
His Throat.
TULARE, April 17.â Judge W. J. Rob
ertson, City Recorder, attempted suicide
by cutting his throat with a razor. He
was found lying on the floor of the kitch
en of his residence by a member of the
household. Medical aid was called in and
his life saved.
Robertson has been in ill health for
several months with little prospect of a
full recovery. No other reason can be
found for his act. as he has been a popu
lar and prosperous official. This year he
refused a nomination to succeed himself.
QUAY'S CASE TO
REACH THE JURY
But Some Rulings Favor
the Defense.
EVIDENCE IS SHUT OUT
BUT AN EXPERT PERMITTED TO
REFRESH HIS MEMORY.
One Decision by Judge Biddle, the
Ex-Senator's Attorneys Claim,
Practically Rules Out Figures
Prior to November, 1896.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
PHILADELPHIA, April 17.â Meyer
Goldsmith, the commonwealth's ac
countant, was on the stand all day in
the trial of Senator Qvay, making
statements of facts that are apparently
damaging to the defendant's side of the
case. Goldsmith is the main witness
for the presecution and it is through
him that all of the documentary evi
dence is to be presented. This wit
ness has examined the bank's books
thoroughly, and he came into court
this morning with a number of type
written sheets containing figures that
he gathered from the various books, re
lating mainly to the deposit of the
State Treasurer and the account of
Senator Quay. He swore to the cor
rectness of his work and then the Dis
trict Attorney formally offered the pa
per in" evidence.
This was objected to by the defense on
the ground that such a paper is not testi
mony. It was insisted that the books
themselves should be placed before the
witness and his statements uttered from
what he saw entered .n them. The Judge
decided in favor of the defense, thus giv
ing that side the first comfort it has ex
perienced sine,, the trial began. The de
fense has apparently gained this point.
The paper was practically in evidence,
Witness Goldsmith using it? contents in
framing his answers to the District Attor
ney's questions. When the Quay lawyers
objected to this they were met by the
Judges ruling that while the paper could
not be used as sworn evidence the wit
ness might use it to refresh his memory.
One effect of all this was to make it
certain that the case will now go to the
jury, there having b^tn some doubt on
this point up to the decision to admit all
that Goldsmith might have to say.
Just before the close of the day's pro
ceedings there was another ruling by
Judge Biddle in favor of the defense which
may have an important bearing on the
futuiv of the case. The statute of limita
tions has been pleaded time and again
by Senator Quay's lawyers as a baj to
much of the testimony presented Ujr the
commonwealth. As Mr. Quay wai Indict
ed on November 17. 1898, the two years al
lowed by the statute for prosecution
would extend hack only to November 17
1896. On this basis Mr. Watson has fre
quently urged Judge Kiddle to exclude
mention of transactions dating back to
1886, when Mr. Quay was State Treasurer.
The court lias uniformly held, in effect,
that the statute of limitations does not
affect the evidence, but does affect the
crime.
The District Attorney pointed out that
he alleges the defendant's guilt during the
two years the law confines him to, and to
prove this the corroborative evidence of
the previous years should certainly be ad
mitted. Judge Blddle decided against the
commonwealth and the Quay lawyers
were very happy. He ruled that the evi
dence should be confined to the conspiracy
alleged and not relate to some other con
spiracy.
It is interesting to note that there was
every indication that the Judge was in
clined to rule against Mr. Quay when
Mr. Watson, who has a suave, persuasive
style, got into another little speech, simi
lar to several he had made during the
day, and begged the court to consider the
promt that the prosecution had so far I
failed to show a specific conspiracy and
that this should be done before it went ;
Into the general question of conspiracy. j
The Judge took this view and ruled ac
cordingly. The lawyers for the defense
claimed that the decision practically rules
out the figures prior to November 17
1896. Mr. Rothermel says its effect will :
be to prevent him from introducing much !
of the evidence he hoped to present.

FAVOBS ANNEXATION.
North Pasadena Would Rid Itself of
Numerous Saloons.
PASADENA, April 17.â Beer and whisky
have become live factors in a movement
for a Greater Pasadena and the annexa
tion of North Pasadena suburb. Water
has long been a factor, but it was never
suspected that the stronger liquids could
be whipped into line. It is thought the
city limits should embrace, as far as pos
sible, those of the water plant, so that no
hardship might be worked upon citizens
by making them bear the expense of out
side consumers of water. But this was
not sufficient incentive for North Pasa
der.ans to support the movement. They
have not believed the ad-vantages would
be as great as the disadvantages.
Now they have changed their minds.
The closing of the "blind pigs" here has
driven the ilk-gal liquor shops to North
Pasadena. The North Pasadenans are
beginning U think that after all annexa
tion and the abolition of the liquor traffic
might be a good thing.

Puget Sound Oysters in Demand.
TACOMA, April 17. -Edward R. Wag
ner of New York is here investigating the
opportunities offered for the shipment of
Pugret Sound Oysters to New York in re
frigerator cars. Mr. Warner was for
merly a Holland oysterman and supplied
the larger cities of Europe. He says the
flavor of Puget Sound oysters more nearly
resembles that of the European oyster
than does any other produced in thig
country. If his plans carry he will select
the largest Sound oysters for shipment
direct to New York and distribution
thence among the larger cities of the At
lantic seaboard.

HAWAII ABOLISHES ITS
CONTRACT LABOR LAW
HONOLULU, April 11.â Just previous
to the departure of the Coptic It is
learned that the Hawaiian Government
has decided tn abolish the contract labor
law. Tho action will be takon to-morrow
morning at a special meeting. It Is un
derstood word came from Washington
to do away with the law, for it is not
reasonable to suppose that President
Dole would nullify a law without the con
sent of President McKinley.
The Immediate cause of the nullifi
cation of the contract labor law Is. the
fact that such a large number of plan
tations have been started within the
past two months. It would require at
least 10,000 additional Japanese for the
new enterprise. This number with the
men already here would practically
Hood the country. President McKinley
has evidently decided to put a stop to
the wholesale Importation of Japanese
coolies.

RAILS FROM SAN
JOSE TO ALVISO
Electric Line to Connect
the Two Places.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SAN JOSE, April 17.â A number of
capitalists of this city have associated
themselves together for the building of
an electric railroad between this city
and Alviso. At their head is ex-Super
visor A. Greeninger. The Board of
Supervisors was to-day petitioned for a
franchise for the road from the north
erly city limits straight out First street
to Alviso. The distance is about eight
miles and it is estimated the railway
could be built, equipped and put into
operation at a cost of from $50,000 to
$75,000. It is proposed to carry freight
on the road.
The hearing of the petition was set
for April 8. The question of the right
of the board to grant a franchise for a
freight-carrying road was referred to
the District Attorney. Should the fran
chise be granted, which is most likely,
a company capitalized at $100,000 will be
immediately organized and the work
rushed.
Now that Congress has appropriated
$48,000 for the deepening and widening
of Alviso Rlough the place promises to
be of importance to San Jose. Already
rights-of-way have been obtained for
a steam railway between Alviso and
this place, for which all the track ma
terials are now stored at Alviso. The
steamers from Alvisn to San Francisco
handle a good amount of freight and
passenger traffic, and a railway should
prove a paying venture from the start.

MRS. L. J. WHEELOCK, Grand Protector of the Order.
THE KNIGHTS
AND LADIES OF
HONOR TO MEET
Annual Convention in
Pythian Castle.
WORK DURING THE PAST YEAR
STRENGTH OP THE ORDER IN
THIS STATE.
Officers Will Be Elected To-Morrow.
Names of the Candidates Who
May Serve for the Next
Term.
At 10 o'clock this morning- the Grand
Lodge of the Knights and Ladies of Hon
or of the California jurisdiction will meet
In Pythian Castle in annual convention,
and will remain in session two days.
During tne past year under the adminis
tration of Mrs. L. J. Wheelock, the grand
protector, the order, which for some years
had been passive, made a wonderful ad
vance, compared with previous years. The
lady devoted a great deal of her time to
the business of the order, enthused the
membership, which seconded her efforts,
and as a result there was a gain of about
13U relief fund members, which brought
that branch up to about 1000. exclusive of
the social members. ., One new lodge was
Instituted, one was consolidated and one
surrendered its charter. The membership
is at this time distributed in seventeen
lodges, with a prospect that before long
several more lodges will be added. There
were fifteen deaths and the beneficiaries
of the deceased members received sums
amounting in the aggregate to about
$2 i, 000.
The grand officers of the order are: Mrs.
Belle W. Conrad of Oakland, junior past
grand protector; Mrs. Lizzie J. Wheelock
of San Francisco, grand protector; Silas
Orr of Sacramento, grand vice protector;
Z. T. Whitten of San Francisco, grand
treasurer; H. W. Quitzow of San Fran
cisco, grand secretary; Mrs. S. Kruger of
San Francisco, grand chaplain; Mrs. B.
Oppenhelmer of San Francisco, grand
guide: Mrs. E. J. Colombe.t of San Jose,
grand guardian; Mrs. [da M. Kidder of
Sacramento, grand sentinel; A. Gompertz
of Sun Francisco, J. A. Colquhoun of Oak
land and Emma T. Parker of Sonora,*
grand trustees; S. American and Mrs.
M'heelock, of San Francisco, supreme rep
resentatives.
Those who are entitled to represent the
order in the Grand Lodge during the cur
rent session are: The supreme and grand
officers named, Past Grand Protectors B.
F. Aiken, J. J. Groom, A. N. Grant, James
P. Dockery, Mrs. U. American. Mrs. M.
E. Carleton, J. C. Plerson, C. C. O'Don
nell, Mrs. Doris J. Kaplan, F. Kemp Van
Ec and Belle W. Conrad; and the following
charter members and representatives: A.

liams, Eugenia R. Kinsman and N. C.
Hearn of Aurora Lodge: H. Friedlander,
G. Wldcler. Antoinette Graff and Emma

The election of officers will be held on
Wednesday morning. Mrs. Wneelock
has been offered a re-nomination, but she
will probably decline to accept the po
sition of State deputy. In that case Z.
T. Whitten, at present grand treasurer,
will be the only candidate for protector.
For the office of vice protector no one
has yet been named. A. Gompertz, at
present one of the trustees, will be nom
inated for treasurer. H. W. Quitzow will

DRAGGED TO PRISON
Lizzie Seaboll, a Pretty
Child, Accused of Steal
ing a Watch From a
Neighbor's House.
LIZZIE SEABOLL, a pretty girl 10
years of age, living at 235 Perry
street, was taken to the City
Prison yesterday on a charg-e of
petty larceny. She Is accused of
stealing a gold watch valued at $20
from Mamie Mangan, a girl 15 years
of age living: at 229% Perry street.
The warrant was obtained in Judge
Graham's court and the only evidence
ag-ainst the little girl is that of a 2
year-old boy, Tommy Mangan, a
brother of the complaining witness.
Mamio Mangan and her mother called
at the office of the Chief of Police yes
terday morning and made a complaint
against Lizzie for stealing the watch,
and they were advised to swear out a
warrant for her arrest.
Lizzie was detained in the City Pris
on from noon till 4 o'clock in
the afternoon, when her father
saw Judge Mogan and the
Judge released her on her own recog
nizance. She denies emphatically the
charge of stealing the watch, and the
Judge was impressed with her story.
"My mother sent me last Wednes
day," she said, "to Mrs. Mangan' a to
borrow the newspaper. While Mamio
and I were talking in the bedroom
Carrie Norton, a big girl who lives

GRAND COUNCIL ROYAL
AND SELECT MASONS
PATRIOTIC REVIEW OF EVENTS
OF THE PAST YEAR.
The Grand Council of Royal and Select
Master Masons of California met yesterday
in thirty-eighth annual session in the Ma
sonic Temple with Grand Master Robert
Ash and all the grand officers at their re
spective stations. There were forty dele
gates present.
The reports of the grand officers that
were submitted showed that the council
is In good condition, numerically and
financially, and that during the year there
was the average Increase in membership.
Aside from the presentation of these re
ports only routine business was trans
acted.
Grand Master Ash in his report made
allusion to the events of the past year,
calling attention to the assassination of
the American sailors on the Maine, the
declaration of war and Its welcome by
the whole people, not in a spirit of re
venge, but in the cause of liberty and hu
manity, and then he said:
The conflict was brief and the American
arms won new victories upon land and sea.
Pome of our companions were the first to draw
the sword and the first to Droclatm the
tidings of victory. Admiral Dewey, our il
lustrious countryman, one of the brightest and
bravest men that wears the stars, less than
<me year ago planted the standard of civiliza
tion and humanity on the Islands of the East
ern sea tn the midst of a victory â the grand
est that history has ever recorded. Soon after

NOTICE. ~~~~
To All Whom It May Concernâ Notice li
hereby given that on the 17th day of April,
1899, ! the Bakerefleld and Los Angeles Rail-
way Company filed its application in writing,
in the office of the Clerk of the Superior
Court of the City and County of San Fran-
cisco, addressed to the Superior Court of said
county, making application for the voluntary
dissolution of the Bakersfield and Los An-
geles Railway Company, which said petition
and application stated that at a meeting of
the stockholders of said company on the 13th
day of April, 1899, colled for the purpose of
passing upon the question, it was resolved to
have a voluntary dissolution of the corpora-
tion, and that such resolution was passed by
a two-thirds vote of all the stockholders and
that all the claims and demands against the.
corporation, of every kind, nature and descrip-
tion, have been fully paid and discharged.
Wherefore, it was ordered by the said Su-
perior Court upon the filing of said petition
and application that the undersigned as
Clerk of said Court, give a notice of such ap-
plication by publication in some newspaper
published in the City and County of San
Francisco, for a period of thirty-five days.
Therefore, all persons having any Interest In
the matter of said application or desiring to
oppose the granting of the same are here-
by notified to file in my office, on or before
the 22d day of May, 1599, any and all objec-
tions which they may have to the granting of
said petition.
In testimony whereof, I have hereunto set my
hand and the official seal of said court this
the 17th day of April. 1899.
(Seal). WILLIAM A. DKANE.
County Clerk and ex-Offlcio Clerk of thÂ«
Superior Court.
By E. M. THOMPSON,
Deputy County Clerk and ex-Offlcio Deputy
Clerk of the Superior Court.

MRS. CLARK MAKES AN ASSAULT
ON HER HUSBAND'S DEFENDERS
Introduction of Her Dead Baby's Shoe in Evidence Causes the
Exciting Scene*
THE trial of Joseph Clark for the killing of Fireman Joseph P. Gross in November last has produced several
dramatic Incidents since its opening, but its only sensation was concentrated into the space of a few minutes
yesterday morning. ' -'.*- i,
Mrs. Clark, whose bitterness toward her accused husband and obvious endeavors to assist the prosecution
ha caused considerable unfavorable comment, seeing in an exhibitâ tiny shoe that once covered her dead
baby's chubby footâ development of a contention favorable to the defendant, made a wild endeavor to pre
vent its introduction in evidence. When the tiny shoe was drawn from its wrappings by Louis P. Boardman, of the
counsel for the defense, Mrs. Clark sprang from her seat beside the District Attorney, sailed toward Mr. Boardman
and H. Digby Johnston, grasped the shoe and shouting, "That's my baby's shoe and you shan't have It here," aimed
a vicious blow at Johnston's head with her left. Johnston ducked with great speed, and the woman's right fist
came toward Boardman's head. Boardman blocked the blow successfully arid was scrambling to get to his feet to bet
ter defend himself when District Attorney Murphy, recovering from his temporary paralysis, superinduced by the
sudden assault on opposing counsel, grasped the frenzied woman by the coat tail and in obedience to a hurried order of
the court assisted the bailiff in depositing her outside the limits of the court room.
The facts which caused the) assault are not without pathetic interest. Clark was put on the stand at the open
ing of the morning session to tell his story of the circumstances leading up to and immediately surrounding the
killing. Evidence had been Introduced to prove that since the death, of his baby girl some years ago his mind was
not normal, and while Clark was on the stand the shoe his dead baby wore was produced that he might testify re
garding it. It was found in one of his bureau drawers after the commission of the crfme. It was wrapped first In a
Bilk handkerchief, then a paper covering, and over all a large towel was tied. On being unwrapped. a bit of verse,
written by the accused, was found tucked between the laces. It reads:
LITTLR JOPIE.
Oh, tiny feet that would not rest.
Oh, dear head pillowed on our breast.
what would we Rive to hold again
The form we lost, 'mid, tears and pain. . Ili^iliilp
Oh. child! the empty cot Is ours.
But thine the sunshine and the flowers.
The shoe and bit of verse were about to be placed in evidence when the woman made the assault. The reason is
apparently plain. That shoe and bit of verse proved the affection Clark held for his family. It. proved that when
he lost his baby sorrow weakened his mind and strengthened the theory of the defense that, when Gross went
Into his home and alienated his wife's affections it was too bitter a blow and culminated in the slaying of the man
that wrecked his home. .. â¢â¢â ,.-â¢ .
Clark in his story went over the details of the shooting. He said that when he fired the fatal shot he was
fearful that Gross was about to make an attempt upon his life, as the 'dead man reached . for his pocket In a
threatening manner. He further told the story regarding the occasion upon which his son was injured and he re-'
ceived a message to that effect. When he reached the house into which the young man had been carried Gross
was there and informed him that his presence was not required. A battle of fists followed and Gross threw the
witness on the floor and was strangling him when they were separated. That Gross was the defendant's bitter en
emy there is no doubt, the reason for which, it has developed, being the relationship existing between Gross* and the
defendant's wife. .iV" .
The testimony of the defendant was largely corroborated by Dr. Green, especially that portion relating to the
assault made upon Clark by the dead man before referred to. After the cross-examination of Clark the case of the
defense rested. Several witnesses on rebuttal were put on the stand by the prosecution and the case had not closed
when the hour for adjournment came. It will close this morning, however, the argument will be made and it is
thought that a verdict will be reached late In the day. >. ? ;i

LOST HER POSITION.
_.
Miss Eliza McLean no Longer an
Army Nurse at the Presidio.
Miss Eliza McLean, who lias acted a?
, chief nurse at the Presidio hospital, has
been dismissed the service. Her friends
have already commenced to aecure her
reinstatement in the position, for which.
they claim, she has every qualification.
In November, IV7, she was employed
at thÂ« city and County Hospital and was
brought into prominence by nursing a ty
phus fever patient at the risk of her life.
When typhoid fever broke out among the
volunteer soldiers at Camp Merrltt she
tendered her si rvices as nurse and did
good work among them.
When the hospital at the Presidio was
completed she and her assistants were
transferred there. Nurses' salaries were
raised by Congress, but before she profit
ed by the increase she lust her position.

GOV. GAGE IS
ON DUTY AT
SACRAMENTO
Has Conference With
Burns on Arrival.
READYTO DISTRIBUTE OFFICES
W. F. FITZGERALD FOR ADJU
TANT GENERAL.
Politicians Are Wondering as to
What Degree of Dan Burns' Dic
tation the Executive
Will Submit.
Governor Gage, refreshed hy a season of
rest, left Los Angeles last Saturday pypn
lnp: on the "Owl" for Sacramento. He ar
rived at the Capitol in due time, whore
he was welcomed by Colonel Burns, while
other disciples of the "push" were wait
ing in the court of the Palace Hotel in
this city to get a glimpse of the State's
executive officer. Some of the malaria
which the Governor took away from Sac
ramento remained with him throughout
his vacation and made his return journey
slightly feverish.
It Is no secret that Gage now intends to
make several appointments, and In conse
quence of this decision on his part there
is some joy in the ranks of the expectant.
When the appointments are announced
there will be wailings of distress, because
thero are at. least ten applicants for
SVi ry desirable position within the gift
of the patronage, distributor. While the
one fortunate recipient will sing the
praises of Gage the other nine will con
signe him to the hottest part of Hades.
The Governor expects that there will be
many disappointments and is prepared to
meet the storm of indignation sure to
follow.
The presence of Burns in Sacramento
when Gage arrived there and the long
private conference that took place after
the executive arrived confirm the suspi
cion that Burns is seeking to dictate the
appointments, and the politicians are
wondering how much dictation Gage will
submit to. Markham stood a good deal
more of Burns than was good for Mark
ham and a vast deal more than was good
for the Republican party of the State of
California. Gage has repeatedly said that
he would allow no one to dictate his ap
pointments and that none of the patron
age at his disposal would be used trf
reward a member of the Legislature for
supporting any candidate for Vnited
States Senator. At Sacramento during
the recent Senatorial contest every legis
lator in the Burns corral, from Dibble to
Cosper, contended that the State patron
age was held in reserve for the followers
of Burns. It is not surprising therefore
that many are anxiously waiting to see
what kind of men Gage appoints to office.
The office of adjutant general, now
filled by General Peeler, does not belong
to the party in the sense that offices are
usually classed. The position is that of
tho Governor's chief of staff, and he will
name some one agreeable to himself. The
executive committee of the Republican
State Central Committee did practically
indorse Colonel George Stone for adjutant
general, and the latter submitted to the
Governor high indorsements from other
sources. Colonel Smedberg and Colonel
CtUTief have very high testimonials re
garding their respective qualifications for
the place. Colonel Seamans of Los An
geles, commander-in-chief of the Re
publican Army and Navy League of Cal
ifornia, is also commended in a fine man
ner. The Governor may not find in this
excellent military material just the man
that suits him, and hence the friendly ad
vices of Colonel Burns may be solicited.
The Governor entertains high regard for
the legal ability of ex-Attorney General
Fitzgerald, and as a knowledge of the
law is useful in the adjutant general's of
fice, Fitzgerald may he selected. The wars
of the National Guard are more likely to
be settled in the courts than on the field
of bloody strife. It happens that Fitz
gerald has had experience In both fields.
He hails from Mississippi, and bears a
good record of service in tho Confederate
army. Governor Stoneman set the exam
ple of appointing an ex-Confederete sol
dier when # he named for his chief of staff
Colonel Cosby. At one time Fitzgerald
and Burns were the closest of friends, but
a disagreement occurred which separated
them. Time works such wonders in heal
ing political wounds that these two na
tive sons of Mississippi may yet clasp
hands under Gage's benediction.
The office of Commissioner of Highways
is classed as a fat, juicy plum. The sea
son has brought forth many aspirants for
the fruit. A. C. Maude of Southern Cali
fornia, who engineered the original
scheme for the creation of the bureau by
an act of the Legislature, is in the field
as an active candidate for Commissioner.
He was in Sacramento when the Legisla
ture convened early In January
and remained there for quite a
length of time. It is said that
he has the indorsement of many
members of the Legislature for the Job.
Senator Bulla was his friend and per
suaded many of the Senators to sign the
petition commending Maude to the Gov
ernor. This incident did not escape the
observation of the "organization." which
was there doing active work to promote
the Senatorial aspirations of Burns. In
seeking for aid and sympathy in a cru
sade for the spoils of office Bulla's friends
cannot wisely look to Colonel Burns.
Somehow Bulla refuses to worship at the
Candelarian shrine, and the colonel be
lieves that Buila represents an ungrateful

SUNNYSIDE AVENUE.
Property-Owners and Residents Com
plain of Their Being Hfemmed
In by Unopened Streets.
Property owners and residents of Sun
nyslde, in addition to the other local ami
necessary Improvements asked by them
for their district, will urge the extension
of Sunnyside avenue from its present ter
mination to the Ingieside racetrack,
where It would join Dewey avenue. The
direct lino of this thoroughfare would run
through a portion of the Sutro land? lying
between the two given points. On the
southern end of this avenue there is only
a short section leading from th" .Southern
Pacilic Raliroad crossing to Islais avenue
to he connected with the Mission road. As
it is now the Sunnyside p< ople ary
hemmed in on both sides, with no way of
getting out except by Chenery street.
Heretofore this avenue of reaching their
homes from the business portion of thÂ©
city was all right in its way, but since
the San Francisco and San Bfateo Rail
road Company has put down a double car
track it leaves the roadway crowded, be
sides there is no way of reaching M
street without loss of time in getting
round by Randall street.
To obviate this the Sunnyside Improve
ment Club will urge the Supervisors to
open Sunnyside avenue from Mission road
to Dewey avenue at as early a period as
the funds of the treasury will Justify, and
in addition place a bridge over the rail
road track at Thirty-fourth and Miguel
streets.
The necessity of a school building in this
portion of the outlying district has been
forcibly presented to both the School Di
rectors and the Hoard of Supervisors by a
special committee appointed by the club
for that purpose. The people also want
electric lights and protection against fire.
At present insurance companies will take
no risks in that portion of the city. A
fire once started means the total destruc
tion of the property attacked. The peo
ple also want some attention bestowed on
the water riuestion ami the grading and
sldewalking of the streets within the dis
trict.

Sudden Death of William Tiedman.
William Tiedman. a native of Germany,
65 years old. died suddenly in the Pres
cott House at 933 Kearny street yesterday
morning. He had been ailing for some
time, and heart disease was undoubtedly
the cause of death. Among his effects
were a bankbook showing $4000 to his
credit and $200 in gold. These were taken
possession of by the Public Administrator.
