THE NEW STATUTE OF FRAUDS.
TO-DAY questions of statutory' construction fall
; ::d fast on the aching heads of newspaper
prietors throughout the State. To-morrow
all articles reflecting upon the dead or assailing the
living are to be signed, in each instance, "by the
true name of the writer." This remarkable specimen
of legislation by which it is proposed to undo the
magni? â  ''le freedom of the press
toward the close of the last century is a joint produc
[ well-known corruptionists, egged on by the
railroad and indorsed by the present Governor. The
estimate of the statute by Governor Gage was tersely
\ hen, in a published interview at Los An
geles, he treated the bare suggestion that he intended
to enter the newspaper business as absurd, for the rea
son that he had signed it, together with the anti
â¢ bill.
:onfessed that the object of this conspir
acy between the legislative and executive departments
of the State government is to gag the press. Like
all similar efforts, it will miserably fail. The breaches
of the constitution to which Grove L. Johnson, Sena
tor Morehonse. Governor Gage and other stung and
sore victims of just criticism were parties have been
already pointed out, and in due time will probably
arrest judicial attention. But it would be amazing, if
purblindness were not an inherent attribute of
malignity, that these astute and aspiring lawyers
I have produced so many questions of interpre
tation. Among them the most difficult to solve is
the meaning of the phrase, "the true name of the
writer." Within the language of the statute a con
stantly recurring question will be, Who is the writer â
the man who composes an article or the copyist? A
local article, tending to blacken the memory of the
dead or to disparage the living, may be the product
of three or four different minds. Their contributions
may have consisted of suggestions so interblended
that it is impossible to segregate them. The language
through which the suggestions are expressed may
have been selected by a person or persons who fur
nished none of the ideas. The words may have been
dictated to an amenuensis. Now, under these condi
tions, which are practical and could be infinitely
varied, who is the writer meant by the statute? We
are free to confess that to the ordinary lay mind this
question seems an unanswerable conundrum. But
more difficult problems can be suggested. Suppose,
for public reasons, that is, "from good motives and for
justifiable ends," it is deemed expedient by a newspa
per proprietor to republish the opinion of a court, in
which shameful misconduct has been imputed, for
example, to an aspirant for the Senate of the United
States. A reporter copies, or two or three reporters
copy, the opinion, or it may be that it is set up from
the volume in which it originally appeared. In this
case who is the writer? Was the opinion written by
the Judge or the bench of Judges who signed it or by
the copyist? Will some of the more learned pro
fessional tools of the railroad answer these ques
tions?
It is conceivable that, upon the grounds already
stated, it might be considered proper to publish a
judgment roll, including the finding and the decree
in a particular case, as, for instance, a suit in Los
Angeles that affects one of our highest State offi
cials. These papers are the composition of the law
yers, the clerk, the process servers and the court.
Who is the writer under the new law?
It might almost be wished that the act should be
operative for a brief period, so that the public might
have an opportunity, by actual examples, to test the
common sense and the legal ability of the legislators
whose agitated nerves and perturbed brains are re
sponsible for such a half-fledged abortion. Its
phraseology is unusually respectful to the dead. Are
Dan P.urns and his supporters included in this cate
gory or will the courts recognize the fact that phy
sically they survive?
Sacramento advices are to the effect that Dan
Burns and Hen Gage held a slate-writing seance last
Sunday in the Governors-o ffice. Portentous rap
pings of these two congenial spirits are said to have
filled with woe the hearts of numerous eavesdropping
job-chasers.
"There are no birds in last year's nest," quotes
the writer of a patent medicine ad., after Longfellow.
No? That's strange! Has there been a recent
wholesale prison delivery at San Quentin?
Senator Perkins has undertaken to contrast Gov
ernor Gape with President McKinley. It is under
stood that California's Senator did uot intend pur
posely to insult the President.
JONATHAN AND JOHN-
*~pHE co-operation in actual war of Englishmen
and Americans at Samoa raises a series of very
* interesting speculations. The ambuscaded
force that was led jointly by an American and a Brit
ish lieutenant was the first allied military organiza
tion of the two countries that has marched and fought
side by side for seven score years. The two lieuten
ants fell together and died side by side, ambushed, as
Braddock was. When their bodies were recovered
the two flags were at half-mast, and the volley over
the dead rattled from the guns of each nation in a
mingled chorus that may echo around the world, in
the track of that shot fired by embattled farmers at
Lexington. The world looks on the scene at Samoa,
and the sober-minded peer into the future for its re
mote meaning. It brings the impression of the
stability of issues, the permanence of national lines
and the fixity of national genius.
About one hundred and forty years ago the policy
of the elder Pitt had matured. Shortly after the ac
cession of George 111 Pitt became aware of the
"family compact" between the Bourbons of France
and Spain, and favored immediate war on Spain and
perpetuation of the Prussian alliance by continuing
the subsidy to Frederick the Great. When his policy
was questioned in the Ministerial Council he declared
his purpose to conquer America in Germany. This
meant that the Prussian alliance was necessary to
enable Great Britain to expel France and Spain from
this hemisphere. The Council, led by Bute, defeated
Pitt's purpose and he retired from the Ministry.
Almost immediately his enemies found it necessary
to begin and carry out his policy, but in doing so
violated his purpose as to Prussia and made an
enemy of an ally. To the war which followed the
American colonies contributed 25.000 soldiers, and
equipped, clothed and paid them. Americans,
among them General Israel Putnam, were at the long
siege and final capture of Havana, The French were
expelled from Canada and the banished Acadians
were scattered. Thirteen thousand of them went to
French Guiana and perished miserably of tropical
diseases, thousands of them dying on DeviPs Island,
where Dreyfus is now a prisoner. When the war
ended England had taken the Canadas, including all
the country north of Ohio. The name of the French
post, Fort Duquesne, had been changed to Pittsburg
in honor of the British statesman whose policy had
prevailed, and the British flag floated over Cuba, the
Philippines, Martinique, Tobago, Dominica, St. Vin
cent, Granada and Minorca. So, a hundred or two
score years ago Americans and British were under
arms together in a war that had in large part the
same objectives as our recent war with Spain, and
against the same enemy we have just now overcome.
When our Revolution began in denial of the right
of taxation without representation, and it became
necessary to hold France in check, George 111 sought
a renewal of the Prussian alliance, but Frederick had
by that time learned to go it alone, and took occa
sion to resent his desertion by Lord Bute by curtly
refusing the overture.
Pitt said he would conquer America in Germany.
England probably lost America in Germany by Bute's
lack of foresight, for, with Frederick as an ally of
England, France could not have spared the fleet
and army which turned the scale and gained for the
colonies victory.
At Samoa John and Jonathan stood side by side
fighting against the policy, the purpose and the na
tive allies of the King of Prussia and Emperor of
Germany, the descendant of Frederick.
The end there is not yet. Xo eye can see far enough
into the future to discern what gains and losses, far
from Samoa, may come from what has happened
there. If war is finally lighted by the fire of Ameri
can and English guns at a common foe, the union of
action between the two nations at Apia will logically
and necessarily continue to the end. With both
against Germany why may not the French dream of
retaking Alsace and Lorraine be realized, and our
Revolutionary ally be rewarded for the gift of
Rochambeau's army and De Grasses fleet through
an alliance with our ancient enemy, which would pun
ish Germany for not holding France in check in 1780
to permit England to defeat our Revolution?
It is an alluring play in history that would punish
Germany for not standing as an ally of Great Britain
against us and reward France for standing as our
ally against Great Britain. The scene includes all
the parties to Pitt's great war, and may mark the
close of an epoch in the history of a race. The centri
fugal force which disintegrated the Anglo-Saxon race
was generated in Grenville's stamp act and proposi
tion to tax Americans without representation. It has
continued in the self-sufficiency of the actual Anglo-
Saxon colonies of Great Britain, for the Canadas,
Australia and New Zealand have assisted the mother
country more by expressions of loyalty than enlist
ment of troops. But the English and American blood
shed in Samoa had not dried in the tropical sun be
fore New Zealand offered assistance to Great Britain
and no doubt set going a sentiment which will spread
through the Australian colonies. The centrifugal
force is spent. Integration of a widespread race
seems to have begun. Where will it lead?
THE TAX SHIRKER'S WRIGGLE.
THE wriggle resorted to by Wells, Fargo & Co.'s
Express Company to escape paying its share of
war taxes exceeds in picturesqueness the twist
ing of the longest and most experienced reptile that
ever played a menagerie star engagement. This
corporation has been beaten in every case so far
brought against it to enforce the law, and it has
been condemned almost unanimously in the court of
public opinion. Not only this, but the point for
which it contends has been decided against it by the
Supreme Courts of several States and one or two
United States Courts.
The law says plainly that express companies shall
stamp their receipts and bills of lading before issuing
them. But these conscienceless corporations through
out the country refuse to do so on purely technical
grounds. An alleged ambiguity in the law has
enabled them to evade bearing their share of the taxa
ttion resulting from the Spanish-Xmerican war.
In this city the wriggle resorted to has degenerated
into a mere trifling with the processes of justice. It
was noted the other day in our local columns that
Wells, Fargo & Co., after being fairly defeated on
jurisdictional points in the United States Circuit
Court, has again caused a case against it to be carried
to that tribunal by writ of certiorari. The issuance
of this writ and the consequent delay can serve no
purpose except to make sport of the United States
Circuit Court. Judge Morrow decided in the Benham
case that he had no jurisdiction to consider the ques
tion as raised in that litigation. Taking the same
case again before him by writ of certiorari can only
result in compelling him to reaffirm his former judg
ment.
The contumacious corporation which is ordering
its attorney to thus travesty justice knows very well
that it ought to pay its war taxes. But under the
leadership of President Valentine it is forcing delay
ftitcr delay, not, perhaps, in the hope that ultimately.
THE SAN FKANCISCO CALL, TUESDAY, APRIL 18, 1899.
the United States Supreme Court will decide the
question in its favor, but for the purpose of saving
as much in war taxes as possible. Every month
during which this corporation succeeds in preventing
a decision against it by a court of last resort in this
State it forces upon the people the payment of be
tween $8000 'and $10,000 which it owes the Govern
ment..
What ought to be done with Wells, Fargo & Co.
is to boycott it. Its resistance to law, to reason and
to common decency is becoming a painful scandal.
Its course in this matter has been unpatriotic and
despicable.
"ONE HUNDRED THOUSAND STRONG.'.
THE CALL, while regretting what is demon
strated to have been the unnecessary outbreak
of hostilities between our troops and the Fili
pinos at Manila, has recognized the necessity of de
fensive measures and has done what it could to in
duce the country to provide such measures in the
ample form required by the situation.
It is not our fault that those who should have
counted the cost of American policy toward the
Filipinos failed to do so. That policy proceeded
rapidly from neglect to exasperation, and the conse
quences are upon us.
General Lawton has made a fruitless expedition,
exhaustive of military energy and resources, to the
south of Manila, protected by gunboats on the La
puna de Bai, and has been compelled to retire and
surrender to the natives the country he conquested.
He found the same problem that is worrying the
military wits of Otis and Mac Arthur, in the swarm
ing numbers of the Filipinos and their matchless ca
pacity for the quick and covert movements of guer
rilla warfare. It is perfectly plain to any one who has
followed military events in Luzon since the 4th of
February that no more permanent results have been
accomplished there than may be attained by drawing
your hand through water. No furrow is left in the
iiuid, which closes in as the obstruction passes. So
the swarms of natives close in behind our regiments
as they march and are ready to ambush and fight
them as they countermarch. With the merciless
torch we have planted desolation where stood the
peaceful homes of those people, but they have seemed
to spring out of the ashes of their huts, gun in hand,
ready to fight again.
Lawton gave it up, and returning to Manila said:
"With the forces I have I could go through the
whole island, but if a government is to be established
it will be necessary to garrison all the towns. It
would take 100,000 men to pacify the islands. I re
gret the necessity of abandoning the captured ter
ritory."
Long ago we warned the imperialists that, having
roused Filipino resentment by indiscriminate
slaughter, there would be no safety in the islands out
side of the garrison posts, and that any relaxation
of military vigilance would be followed by uprisings
and massacres. We have roused a native hatred more
bitter than that against Spain, for she never prom
ised independence nor justified a hope of self-govern
ment. We did, and have followed bitter disappoint
ment with a slaughter in two months of more ghastly
proportions than Spain was guilty of in two cen
turies.
It is worth while to follow Lawton's frank regret
at the necessity of abandoning the territory he had
captured by reading the evident afterthought of the
War Department at Washington. The acting ad
jutant general is quoted as saying that Lawton's ex
pedition accomplished its purpose, which was to dis
tribute "dodgers" on which the proclamation of the
American Commissioners was printed. The depart
ment adds: "Considerable difficulty has been ex
perienced by the commission in acquainting the na
tives of the kindly intentions of this Government."
Therefore Lawton was sent with gunboats and regi
ments, shelling and shooting as they went, "to spread
broadcast the recent proclamation of the Philippine
Commission, and before his departure he was given
careful instructions as to the line of conduct he
should pursue, and he has evidently carried them
out in a satisfactory manner." Then why does Law
ton regret the necessity of abandoning the captured
territory, if he was not sent out to capture it, but
went merely as a bill-sticker?
This evident afterthought of the War Department
will deceive nobody. The adventure in the Philip
pines has developed a wretched situation. The equal
izing effects of disease and the climate, upon which
The Call has ceaselessly insisted, have not been reck
oned with by our Government. The list of dead, wound
ed and sick American soldiers in that tropical climate
lengthens day by day. They are martyrs to a funda
mental error committed at home, to the sap-pated
enthusiasm of imperialism and the misleading of pul
pit, press and politicians. With the history of the
South Sea bubble and Law's Mississippi scheme be
fore us we have paid $20,000,000 for the same old
gold brick, and the 100,000 men required to hold it
will cost us $200,000,000 a year.
If things continue as they are tending now it is the
plain duty of the President to call Congress together
and consult the representatives of the people. The
costly measures now in action are not within the
executive scope. The war with Spain is officially
finished; it began by the Congressional declaration
of war. But without any such declaration we are in
a war that is costing ten times as much in lives and
money. It cannot go on much longer without seek
ing council with the people.
The statement from Washington that General
Lawton's expedition from Manila was designed, not
to subdue the Filipinos, but "to spread broadcast
throughout the territory traversed by the American
troops copies of the proclamation issued by the
Philippine Commission several weeks ago," throws
a new light on the subject. The press has been treat
ing the General as a soldier, but the Government
seems to have regarded him as general bill-poster.
The Chief of the New York Fire Department says
the only moral to be drawn from the destruction of
the Windsor Hotel and the Andrews mansion is that
heavy draperies about windows are dangerdus and
ought to be suppressed. If you wish to prevent peo
ple from seeing into your parlors pull down the
blinds.
Now that another legislative committee is investi
gating the Tammany administration of New York
City, it is worth while for some antiquarian to dig
over the political graveyard of the State and find out
where that old Lexow Committee is buried and what
sort of epitaph is written on the tomb.
As the Earl of Dunraven has just been elected a
member of the Limerick Council, it is evident he is
still capable of making a winning race in Irish poli
tics, but it is safe to say he won't try yacht-racing
any more.
As General Alger will not resign, nor General
Miles retire, the beef inquiry board will have to make
a very straight and narrow report if they wish it to
pass without hitting somebody in authority*

a month. His hours of duty are fixed

o'clock in the morning. This arrangement
will provide a tender for the bridge all of
the time during the twenty-four hours
and will meet the protest recently made
by shipping men as to charges made for
opening the draw after 0 o clock in the
evening.

Something of a breeze was created by
an objection offered by Supervisor Per
rault to the opening of the bids that were
called for some tlmo ago for furnishing
the Hall of Justice. In support of his
objection he stated that in his opinion
the specifications prepared by the city s
architect were not so drawn as to per
mit the city reaping any advantage from
the competition of bidders. He also
thought that many of the things called
for in the furnished specifications were
more properly chargeable to the bund
ing account. He asked that the opening
of the bids be postponed for one week In
order to give members who wished to do
so an opportunity to investigate the mat
ter and ascertain the facts. A motion to
this effect was carried, Aigeltinger, At
tridge, Holland and Kalben voting no.

nance recently passed and which had the
effect of closing the Ingleside track.

the world, arrived last nigrht from the
Orient with his wife and registered at the

W. A. BisFcll, assistant manager of the
Santa Fe, left last night for New York
City, where, -with other California rail
road representatives, he will meet with
the agents of all the transcontinental and
trunk lines in a conference over transcon
tinental transportation and rates.
Manuel Morales Tobar, who was Min
ister of Foreign Affairs and the Interior
at Guatemala during President Barrios'
administration, is at the Occidental with
his secretary, Manuel Saenz. Mr. Tobar
is the adviser of President Cabrera, who
recalled him from the exile to which Bar
rios had subjected him about a year ago.
He has been visiting this country with
a view to establish closer commercial re
lations with the United States and hl3
country, and will return home In two
weeks.

Track Notes.
Jimmy Powers, the rider of Jim Prownell in
the final race of the day, was told by the
judges to take a vacation for the balance of
the. meeting. He was blamed for causing the
bumping match a furlong from the wire.
Bookmaker Caesar Young and crew departed
for St. Louis last evening.

only this winter, also left for the Eastern coun
try yesterday.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, TUESDAY, APRIL 18, 1899.
INQUEST UPON
CORRIGAN'S NEW
MORGUE VICTIM
Why Patrick McDer
mott Drank Poison.
LOST HIS ALL ON 0..E RACE
PREFERRED A SUICIDE'S GRAVE
TO THE ALMSHOUSE.
Game to the Last, He Avoided His
Friends Who Had Expected to
Hear of His Good
Luck.
T';i trick McDermott, the latest Morpue
victim of Flimflam Corrlgan, the Death's
H' ;il and Cross Bones of the Pacific
Coast Jockey Club, was the subject of a
Coroner's inquest yesterday.
Poor McDermott had passed the three
score years and ten reputed to be the
lot of man. In his old age he found him
self out of employment and as his meager
savings out of his meager earnings began
to melt away he made up his mind, as
he had clone often before, to stake his
last f<w dollars on the races. So he went
to Oakland last Friday and was taken in
hand by one of Corrigan's touts, who
picked out for him a quod horse, but
one not slated to win by the Corrigan
eurjis of bunko operators.
MeDermott's horse waa behind at the
start, and then steadily gained on the
six ahead of him. He was third at the
half mile, having worked his way up
three places; at the three-quarters he
was second and driving the lewd horse at
a fast utvl furious gait. McDermott stmid
t'> win $100 on an investment of $20 and
those who saw the old man at that stage
<>i" the race say that lie threw his hat into
the air and shouted himself hoarse with
joy.
Then the horse that the gamblers had
put up to win forged ahead from fourth
place and poor McDermott'- hopes were
battered to death with every clatter of
the hoofs.
Sick at heart the old man returned, but
he wore an aspect of gayety. Although
he had not a dollar tn the world ami the
prospect of the almahouse before him he
was game to the last. He smiled in the
faces of his acquaintances and told them
that the horse upon which he had wagered
his all on a confidential tip had lost. But
lie avoided his most intimate friends, went
to the stable at s;Â»0 Bryant street, swal
lowed a potion of carbolic acid and lay
down To die.
James Brady, a teamster residing: at 3'H>
Seventh street, saw McDermott to the
Oakland ferry boat and parted from him
after wishing him good luck. On Friday
evening he visited McDcrmott's usual
haunts for the purpose of inquiring as
to his pood or ill fortune, but failed to
lind his friend. Suspecting that it was
another Corrigan case, he went to the
Btable at 7 o'clock on Saturday morning
and found his friend dead.
Coroner Hill asked Brady whether he
had ever observed any Indications of un-
Iness of mind on the part of Mc-
Dermott, and Brady replied that he
never had.
Matthew Smith, a capitalist residing at
266 Page street, was called to testify as
' I'lrnwtts mental condition. He
testified that it was sound.
"One reason why I have asked this
question," said th.> Coroner, "is that the
papers have stated that McDermott was
playing the racÂ» b. a verdict of tempor
ary insanity should be brought in in the
case of any one who hucks the racop."
"I never bet a quarter on a horse race
in my life," protested Capitalist Smith.
That remark perhaps explains why
Matthew Smith is a capitalist.
The jury brought in a verdict of sui
cide.
A COURSING MATCH.
Motto's Owner Will Run His Charge
Against Mercy May if Grace
Will Judge.
For pome days past the topic of con
versation among le-ashm^n is the pro
posed match race between Motto and
Mercy -May. The flnjrs have already met
on the coursing sward, but it appears
that their respective owners will n<>t be
convinced as to which is the better dog
until the "flyers" meet in a contest of
besi two in three courses. The owner of
Motto is evidently willing to run his
charge provided that the owner of Mercy
May will consent to have the trial's
judged by John Grace, as the followiim
i'tt, r will attest:
MOUNTAIN VIEW, April 17. 1899.
Sporting Editor Callâ Dear Sir: There has
been so much fuss made over the proposed
Motto-Mercy May match race that all I win
ask you i.Â« to be. so kind as to publish the fol
lowing challenge in your valuable paper: I
will run my bitch Motto against Mercy May
for $100 or $500 In Union Park or In any other
park if John Grace will judge and James Grace
will slip. Every coursing man knows that
when there Is money up on a match race that
Mr Grace Is the only man in this country who
ran Five satisfaction, and all coursing men
know that the best flog will win. Now, If the
owner of Mercy May will leav* a deposit of
$100 In your hands I will meet Mr. Hobbs. the
owner of Mercy May, and make the match for
any day or any place he names. Those two
dogs met In the final at Union Park a few
weeks ago, and every coursing man present
thought that Motto won the course, but Judge
Bull Rave Mercy May the flair, hence the pro
poped match race. Hoping you will publish
this, I remain, yours sincerely,
FRANK H. McCOMBE.
MILITARY OR CIVICP
Captain Dillon Prefers a Court-Mar
tial to Judge de Haven.
Captain John G. Dillon of the Govern
ment transport Morgan City, Indicted for
knocking down a Bailor on the high seas
with the butt cud of a Mauser rifle, ap
peared before United States District
Judge de Haven yesterday morning 1 and
asked to have his bail reduced from $1000
to $600. The court declined to grant the
request. Then counsel for the defendant
raised the point that as the offense was
alleged to have been committed on board
a Government transport the United States
courts had no jurisdiction and that the
offense, if any, was triable only before a
court martial. Â»
Argument on this point will be heard
this morning.

"SOCIETY IS CRYING
BEFORE IT IS HURT"
LITTLE EGYPT" is authority for the fact that the representative matrons
of the swell set are crying out long before they are hurt. Also that their
fears for the safety of their gilded masculine darlings are entirely
groundless, and if anybody is in need of protection it is she herself.
"Little Egypt's" claims to fame are based solely on the facts that the
diminutive danseuse was the cause of a sensational raid at the now famous
Seeley dinner and that she considers herself the only exponent of muscle
dancing in America.
"Egypt's" claim to the attention of the ultra fashionable set is based upon
the man3 r rumors that have been going around to the effect that the mascu
line pets of swell circles are seeking to emulate the famous Seeley and are
giving dinners at which "Little Egypt" is of even greater importance than the
wine that circulates and chet-rs.
Thus it comes to pass that morning sessions in dainty boudoirs are of daily
occurrence ami the discussions are of "suppression," coupled with the horrible
revelations a raid might engender.
"We must protect our innocent boys," say the matrons, "and oh, if there
Should be a raid and the poor fellows' names get into the papers, and if our
sweet, innocent girls should know" â
"Little Egypt," who is causing allthe worry, laughs at society's discom
fort and vows they are crying out before they are hurt.
"I have only been t<> one dinner," said "Little Egypt" yesterday, "and all
this talk is all bosh. If they want to got me to dance at their dinners it id go
rag ?>> coal them high, and 1 do not believe the dudes have the price. The
other day a society lady came to me and offered me $100 to dance at her din
ner. '1 only dance two minutes for JIM,' I told her. My price is $500, and
besides 1 have to know that I am dancing for the right kind of people, for
a girl in my position has to be careful where she goes.
"As for the society women trying to suppress me," the muscle dancer
continued, "that is ridiculous. Not a day goes by but I hear of society wo
men who are anxious to take dancing lessons of me. 'All right,' I say to
them, 'all right; I'm willing If you can prove your character and pay my
prieiV Further than that, they are all crazy to see me, and every evening the
boxes of the theaters where I dance are crowded with society women who, se
curely veiled, hide behind their box curtains and watch me dance."
"Little Egypt" is convinced that all the talk of dinners is the outcome of
jealousy, and that society ladles are hurt because she will not condescend to
meet them unless they can "prove their character."
As for the poor youths that the matrons and chaperons would protect,
"Little Egypt" only laughs at them. "Protect the boys?" she said. "If the
women would stop to think they would find it is I that need protection."
POLICE DEPARTMENT
GOES ON THE STAND
THE INGLESIDE CASE BEFOBE
JUDGE MUBASKY.
Ach Tries to Prove That Force Was
Committed and Business of Track
Was Injured by Police.
The case of the Pacific Coast Jockey
Club versus Lees came up before Judge
Murasky yesterday afternoon Garret
McEnerney and District Attorney Murphy,
the attorneys for the defendant, were un
able to be present, and in their absence
John A. Hosmer conducted the trial for
the defendant.
Henry Ach. attorney for the plaintiff
corporation, 'put a small army of gate
keepers and officers of the law on the
stand. He tried to draw out of all of
them that force had been committed in
effecting an entrance Into the racing park,
and that the diminution in attendance
consequent upon the interference of the
pullce was due to that very fact and not
because betting was prohibited.
All the high dignitaries of the Police
Department were given a chance to tes
tify and relate their part In the action
which resulted In the closing of the Ingle
side trn'k. Captains Wittman and Gll
len Chief I.ces. Officers Welch and Whe
lan and Sergeant Helms gave their testi
mony, whli-h agreed in the main that an
entrance was effected on the several days
without any violence. "Whelan testified
that when he attempted to enter the gate
Henry Ach was standing there, and that
the attorney said there would be no forci
ble opposition made to the entrance of
W. Uii but what he wanted was a test
case Ach denied having made any such
statement and went on the stand to dls-
Pln order to bring out the fact that the
falling off in attendance was Ale to tho
presence of the police on the grounds,
Ach .ailed Colin M. Smith, who readily
admitted that his only occupation was
that of being a capitalist; C. C. Hardy of
the California Wine Association and a
memb'T of the Olympic Club, and J. J.
Callunden. the superintendent of the.
Morse Detective Agency. Smith and
Hardy claimed not to be betting men,
but confessed to a love ror horseracing.
They said they would not go to the track
when it was ornamented by rows of po
licemen.
Charles Farson and Walter M. Tilton.
gate-keepers, recited how the police made
their entrance Into the Ingleside track.
Callunden was the last witness on the
stand, and the court adjourned after his
direct examination by Ach. The case was
then adjourned until this morning at 10
o'clock, when Hostner will begin his cross
examination of this witness.
SCENIC ROAD'S INNOVATION.
Time of the Evening Train Material
ly Shortened.
An Innovation on the San Francisco and
North Pacific Railroad was inaugurated
yesterday which will bring relief to the
through passengers on that line. Here
tofore train 18, which reaches San Fran
cisco at 6:20 p. m.. has stopped at every
little way station to take on milk, and
this constant stopping and consequent
delay have been a source of much an
noyance to the patrons. Yesterday this
was all changed, and now No. 16 comes
through from Petaluma with but three
stops and time has been shortened twenty
minutes from Ukiah, the train leaving
that point at 1:20 instead of 12:40 o'clock
as before. A special milk train runs from
Ignacio and relieves the regular passen
ger train of the burden. A special is also
fun from Tiburon to San Rafael ahead
of No. 0. which leaves at 3:30 p. m. This
change was necessitated by the greatly
increased travel over the scenic road dur
ing the summer and will be especially
pleasing to the devotees of golf who go
to the "links at Wagners.

Lecture by "lan Maclaren."
Dr. John Watson, "Tan Maclaren," who
will give a farewell lecture at Sherman,
("lay & Cp.'fl hall on Friday afternoon at
half-past 3 o'clock, is the pastor of Sefton
Park Church In Liverpool, England, the
finest church In that city, and that Is con
stantly crowded hy one of the largest and
most influential congregations in all Eng
land. As a prearhor, as well as an au
thor. Dr. Watson holds a foremost place.
He Is a speaker of extraordinary force
and clearness, and a man of great elo
quence and originality of thought. He is
the lntelkctu.nl attraction of Liverpool,
and Is even more successful as a render
of his writings than he Is as a preacher.
It is certain that no man who has ever
visited this city has so charmed with his
rr ad ings as did Dr. Watson when he ap
peared at the California Theater a fort
night ago. Upon the occasion of this his
fare-well appearance, he will give "The
Life of Dr. William McClure" complete.
and selections from his latest and unpub
lished story. Seats will be on sale at the
music store of Sherman. Clay & Co. to
morrow morning at popular price's. Chil
dren will he admitted for half price.
Innocent of Theft.
Mrs. Ann Riley. a respectable widow,
| who makes a living for herself and ehll
j dren by doing chores In some of the stores
on Market street, was presented with a
| cape by Joe Rosenberg, 816 Market street,
: six weeks ago. me cape had been left in
Rosenberg's waiting room about a year
! ago, and as no one called for it Mrs. Riley
was presented with it. She had just left
â â  Rosenberg's store yesterday afternoon
when a lady accused her of stealing her
cape, claiming that the one Mrs. Riley
was wearing belonged to her. She called
; a policeman, and Mrs. Riley was booked
; at the City Prison for petty larceny, but
i was immediately released "on her own
recognizance by Judge Mogan.

caufced an early cessation of business in canned

NOTICE TO MARINERS.
A branch of the United States Hydrographic.
Office, located In the Merchants" Exchange, Is
maintained In San Francisco for the benefit of
mariners without regard to nationality and
free of expense.
Navigators are cordially invited to visit the
office, where complete sets of charts and sail
ing directions of the world are kept on hand
for comparison and reference, and the latest
Information can always be obtained regarding
lights, dangers to navigation and all matters
of interest to ocean commerce.
The time ball on the tower of the new Ferry
building at the foot of Market street is hoisted
about ten minutes before noon and dropped at
noon, 120 th meridian, by telegraphic signal re
ceived each day from the United States Naval
Observatory, Mare Island, Cal.
A notice stating whether the ball was dropped
en time or giving thÂ« error, if any. is published
in the morning papers the following day.
C. G. CALKINS,
Lieutenant. U-: S. N.. In charge.

The story of Mr. Sachs' disappearance
is a. common one. He bought wine for
brunette ladies on a blonde intfoine ana
wasted what was left of his monthly pay
as salesman at making poor guesses on
the dogs at the coursing parks. Incident
ally he sandwiched in meteoric and dollar
marked flights through the tenderloin and
the denizens of that strict are his chief
est mourners. Sachs also took an oca
sional hand at cards and it is said that
a numb. of the cleverest dealers in town,
relying upon the high financial standing
of his uncle and employer, are out some
money on the credit they extended him.
The Sachs Brothers, by whom the ab
sent man was employed, deny that he was
short in his accounts. He was employed
only as a salesman and had nothing what
ever to do with the cash of the firm. They
make little of his alleged disappearance
and are inclined to think that it is sim
ply one phase of their nephew s ideas of
a good time. Â . â¢ .
Sachs has always been allowed great
latitude by his employers, by whom he
has been employed since he left college,
and taking advantage of this ha es
tablished for himself the. reputation of
one of the best things in the tenderloin.
He has not lived within his salary for the
past year, but the difference was made
up either by outstanding credits or hon
ored drafts" on his employers, and they
are Inclined to believe that their missing
clerk will report for duty again in a day
or so.

THE STORY OF THE
BIG CARDINAL AX
o o
O T~\ ERKKLKT, April 17.â A huge, blazing: bonfire, n hand, a big red ax, ROO <J
O r-\ college mon walking in lock step, a throng of co-eds on the bleachers, ft
q | J the wildest, kind of excitement, aperfect pandemonium of yells, cheers, ~
j hoots and howlsâ such, In outline, was the campus of the University of
O California this morning. O
O The ax occasioned It all. It was a Stanford ax, painted with a pood O
1 q thick coating of Stanford red. This was the ax captured at the baaeball game ft
last Saturday. o
** This morning shortly before the hour for drill the trophy was brought J?
O upon the cam] us under a strong guard of six-footers. Tt was like a call to Â©
.O arms. Instantly i . dressed In their cadet uniforms, came running O
q fmm all directions, Practically the whole university. Instructors and all, for- ft
( q Book their work to Join the mob around the cardinal's stolen mascot, sus- q
v pended aloft on a high pole. No one thought of drill. The ehout for a rally if
O went up and forthwith a rally was held. Â©
O The formalities were few and simple. Willing hands made short work of O
q board walks, freshmen dragged logs from a big lumber pile, boughs of trees O
q and tins of coal oil were added, and within less time than It takes to tell, a ft
I huge bonfire stood Ir. the middle, of the lower campus. if
jO Headed by the band, all the men, 800 strong, fell into one long line, and, Â°
! O walking in lockstep Single file, went up and down the campus, singing, cheer- Â©
! Q ing and yelling In a way wnich Berkeley has never before known in its his- O
i q tory. The procession ended by forming a ring around the bonfire, and there ft
*~ the whole crowd danced around the blazing pile, howling until their lungs ~
Â° could Ftand it no longer. It was an impressive exhibition, carried out on a
O large scale, and will be handed down as a tradition among Berkeley collegl- O
q ana for years to come. ft
California intends to hold to its stolen trophy. It is rumored that a spe- ft
Â° clnl officer of the Associated Students will be appointed, to be known as cus- X
! O todian of the ax. Accompanied by a strong bodyguard, it will be his duty to
; O carry it to field-days and football matches as an incentive to college rivalry. Â©
rt There are likely to be some lively scrimmages on such occasions if the Stan- O
*Z ford men try to take it away. ~
Th" cardinals made an attempt to recover their mascot by strategy this o
O afternoon. A shabbily dressed man on horseback called at the house In

THE SAX FRANCISCO CALL, TUESDAY, APRIL 18, 1809.
WILL NOT BE
A PARADE ON
FABIOLA DAY
Lady Managers Have
Fixed the Date.
FLOWERS WILL BE USED
EACH PARTICIPANT IS TO ACT
INDEPENDENTLY.
One Week From Next Saturday Is
Set Apart for the Benefit of the
Hospital â Big Results
Expected.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, April 17.
After a consultation with Thomas A.
Williams Jr., of the Emeryville race
track, the lady managers of Fabiola
have agreed upon Saturday, April 29, as
Fabiola Day.
The ladies have discussed the advisa
bility of having a floral parade on that
day as in former years, and have arrived
;ii the conclusion that such a display is
undesirable. On past occasions there has
been much disappointment and not a llt
tlo unpleasantness provoked on account
of the disposition of prizes in the various
classes of rigs or in the matter of prece
dence in the procession. This year every
body will be at liberty to act independ
ently, and Fabiola Day will be literally
a go-as-you-j lease affair.
it is verj probable that tho display on
April 29 will be more generous than on
other occasions, for every one, it is ex-
Parted, will make some effort at decora
tion, and certainly the colors of Fabiola
will be lavishly used.
"The matter has been thoroughly dis
cussed," said Mrs. John Yll'.f>. president
of Fabiola Association, and we nave de
cided to center all our efforts upon in
creasing the attendance on Fabiola Day
and leaving all matters of decoration or
Horal display to the individual tastes of
our friends. , _
"We feel that no apology is needed for
accepting the generous offer of Mr. \\ l\
liams, nr.d we hope that Fabiola will re
ceive as greal a benefit from its annual
Derby Day as in past years. During the
past year there was some difference of
opinion regarding the propriety of
la Day at Emeryville, but we reel
assure. l that when a wounded or sirk
human being is having his sufferings al
leviated no <>ne stops to consider whether
the means by which such benefits are
made possible come from Mr. Williams or
somebody else."
As usual, an extra liberal purse will be
hung up for the Fabiola Derby, and
should the day be fine there will no doubt
i attendance, as Saturday
is always the most popular day at the
track. ' .

AFTER MANY YEARS
THE ACCIDENT CAME
DRUNK and sober, for twenty
live years Michael Weaver,
tanner, night after night stag
gered or walked along the
plank that bridged the big vats
in the Horn tannery at the cor
ner of Vermont and Army streets,
and never an accident befell him un
til last Sunday night.
Rendered careless by familiarity
with his narrow walk and the frown-
Ing vats of tan solution that yawned
beneath his feet and being in his
usual bedtime condition of hilarity, hf
lost his balance and fell.
"BUCK" DRAWS
CURTAINS AND
SHOWS HIS RAND
Fires His First Gun in
the Thirty- First.
DISTRICT CLUB ORGANIZED
THE BLIND BOSS PLOTS TO CON
TROL DEMOCRATIC PARTY.
His Henchmen Will Attempt to
Organize Powerful Clubs in
All Sections of the
City.
Chris Buckley, the deposed political
boss, has commenced to lay the founda
tion of a political structure upon which
he hopes to boost himself into power at
the coming camp;ugn. The firFt brick in
the flimsy structure was laid last night,
in the Thirty-first District, long since the
banner district of local Democracy, and
few of the unsuspecting citizens who
signed the roll contributing their mite to
"Buck's" power dreamed that they were
being passed a nicely handled gilded cube.
Ever ainco "Dickie" Doolan, who was
a candidate at the last election for Super
intendent of Streets, but who la "running
yet." and Charles Pomposity Gallagher,
who was dragged Into prominence by the
Committee of One Hundred, have been
manipulating the political affairs of the
district there has been more or less dis
satisfaction among the active workers of
the district, and the blind boss figrured
that the Thirty-first would be a star sec
tion in which to plant the first seeds of
his plot to control the approaching pri
mary.
Several of Buck's faithful lambs decided
to hold a meeting at Hibernia Hall on
Ninth street last Monday night, but when
they charged en masse on the hall direct
from Con Harrigan's corner and Alec
Greggains' saloon the Janitor refused to
allow them to meet there, because he
feared they would pack off the piano or
singe the leaves of the wreath around
the picture of St. Patrick.
They succeeded, however, In renting
Teutonla Hall, and the outfit met there
last night to go through the process of
organization.
Aiec Ureggains sent up a horde of
stutters from his disihict to boost Buck's
game along and they all had orders to
"land on" the first voter who attempted
to buck the programme. Ex-Senator Den
nis McCarthy was slated for chairman,
but as his everlasting devotion to Buckley
was well understood throughout the dis
trict the push came to the conclusion at.
the last moment that such a move would
be a trifle "raw," so Dennis was forced
to give way to another.
When the meeting was called to order
by Cass LeClare the word was passed
around that the powers that be had de
cided to elect Joe Keating chairman of
the organization. Keating, of course, held
a shotgun title to the job and was elected
without opposition. Then "Sprat" Mc-
Kenna nominated "Jack" Sullivan for sec
retary. Sullivan was also elected without
opposition and the organization from
which Buckley expects so much was com
plete.
There were many shrewd politicians in
the district who saw through the deal and
they refused to attend the meeting or
even allow their names to be usea to
foster the plans of the man from Liver
more. Many others who were inveigled

NOTICE.â Bidders will estimate and bid
a price for each inmate per day (not to
exceed twenty-five cents in the aggre-
gate)â the word "inmate" as used in-
cludes all employes of and in the insti-
tution named, as well as all prisonersâ
with the distinct understanding that such
price will bind the bidder to furnish all
the articles and supplies specified in the
foregoing schedule, of the kind and qual-
ity as shown by said samples, and deliver
the same at all places In the city and
county wherein prisoners are confined, as
required, without extra charge from the
price bid, and that in no event will the
city and county be -liable for any rations
furnished in excess of the total number of
inmates. Also, that the quality of the ar-
ticles and supplies called for, when not
designated to be of the best quality, and
all supplies furnished the County Jails,
Nos. 1, 2 and 3, or City Prison, to be sub-
ject to the "approval of the Sheriff or
Chief of Police, respectively, whose de-
cision shall be final and conclusive as to
the reception or rejection of any or all
of the supplies offered; and said Sheriff or
Chief of Police, by and with the consent
of the Chairman of the Committee on
Health and Police, shall have the power
to purchase any or all of the supplies or
articles required at the market rate, if
not furnished of the kind and quality as
shown by samples, at the expense of the
party to whom the contract may be
awarded, if the said party fails to fur-
nish the quantity or quality of the sup-
plies or articles designated which under
the contract he may be required to fur-
nish. â 
Also, bidders will estimate under the ex-
press understanding and agreement that
under no consideration will the Board
recognize or allow any assignment of any
contract, or allow the supplies included
in any contract to be furnished from or
by any other person than the person to
whom the contract is awarded, cither un-
der an assignment .or other instrument,
unless the consent of the Hoard of Super-
visors is first obtained, through a Resolu-
tion, duly passed by said Board and ap-
proved by the Mayor, consenting and i
agreeing to the same.
Further, If the number of inmates shall
be less during the continuance of said
contract than the number herein esti-
mated, the price bid and the actual num-
ber of inmates shall be the only basis on
which demands shall be presented, allow-
ed or paid, the value of all supplies not
furnished to be deducted at the market
rates from any amount due the contrac-
tor at such time as the Committee on
, Health and Police may determine.
All the articles of food must be of
sound and wholesome quality, 'and sub-
i ject to the Inspection and approval of the
Mayor, Board of Health, Physician of
the City and County Jail and the Com- I
mittee on Health and Police of the Board
of Supervisors, and all of said supplies \
to be inspected and weighed at such
times and places as may be required by
the Committee on Health and Police.
The party to whom the contract is
awarded will be required TO IMMEDI-
ATELY FURNISH THREE SAMPLES
OF EACH OF THE NON-PERISHABLE
FOOD SUPPLIES CALLED FOR AND
ENUMERATED IN THIS SCHEDULE
AND DELIVER THE SAME TO THE
CLERK OF THE BOARD OF SUPER-
VISORS. All food supplies shall be sub-
mitted for inspection by the person, firm
or corporation so furnishing the same at
one of the public food stations estab-
lished by the Board of Health of this
city next to the Municipal Institution to
be furnished with such food supplies and
the character and quantity and quality
of such food supplies shall be then and
there first inspected and said Board of
Health shall make report of such inspec-
tion forthwith to the Board of Super-
visors.
The party to whom this contract is
awarded will be required, prior to or at
the time of the execution of said con-
tract, to pay the cost of advertising this
notice in three dally newspapers, and to
also pay the cost of the publication in
the official newspaper of the Resolution
awarding him the contract.
In order to preserve uniformity and to
facilitate the award, the Board of Su-
pervisors has resolved to receive no bids
unless made upon blank forms prepared
by the committee, and a certified check
on some bank in the City and County of
San Francisco for the sum of two thou-
sand ($2000.00) dollars, in favor of and
made payable to the clerk of the Board
of Supervisors, must be deposited by the
bidder with his bid, conditioned, if the
proposal is accented, and the contract
awarded, and if the bidder shall fail or
neglect to execute the contract, pay the
printing charges and give the bond re-
quired within six days after the award
is made, then and in that case the said
sum shall bo paid into the City and Coun-
ty Treasury by said Clerk as liquidated
damages for such failure and neglect.
Blanks furnished by the Clerk.
The Board reserves the right to reject
all bids if the public good so require.

UNDER ARREST BUY
SILL CHANCE OF
ANY PUNISHMENT
INQUIRY INTO
THE RIOTIIG OF
SUNDAY NIGHT
But Little Result Ex
pected.
IT RESTS WITH THE MEN
REHFELD TO TRY TO IDENTIFY
THE OFFENDERS.
The Suspects Will All Be Paraded Be
fore Him To-Bayâ He Is Not
Sure of Even One of
the Mob.
A groo4 part of the garrison of the Pre
sidio is under arrest as a result of the
riot a.t the grates of the post Sunday even-
Ing. The arrest is only a constructive
one, however, for there is but one in
durance vile, and he is confined for the
row of Saturday night, and not for that
of Sunday; the other two hundred and
more have had their names and compa
nies taken down, and there the matter
will rent until they have all passed before
the owner of the ruined saloon so he may
Identify those he saw in the affray. They
will aJI have to tell where they were Sun
flay night, and if their answers be satis
factory they will have to be let go.
The investigation has been placed in the
hands of Lieutenant D. B. Devore, who
Js one nf tho officers in charge of the
laltien," troops enlisted into provis
ional companies and awaiting assignment
[es in the regiments now being
ted !:. foreign service. The casual
ties were the troops engaged in the affair
of Sunday night.
The investigation is not likely to be an
interesting one. There is no evidence
against the men except what Rehfeld. the
â ! m;in, can give, and at the time of
the assault on his place most of his ener
n securing safety for him
self instead of evidence for any investi
gation that might follow. The men who
were in the mob art- not known and they
will not come forward to identify them
selves, and there is but one sergeant who
stands a chance of being brought up to
any danger of detection. He is an infantry
sergeant Rehfeld saw in the crowd around
his saloon. The sergeant was not throw
ing any stones m>r was he making any
noise or trouble, but he was in the crowd,
and the saloon man thinks he can recog
nize him if he sees the man again. When
Rehfeld tries to give a description of the
sergeant, however, hischance of ever iden
tifying him seems to be dim and distant.
The officers themselves have very little
hope of ever bringing any one to trial for
mobbing the place. The casuals are all
new men and not one in five is known to
the commanders. They are arriving and
departing all the time and a personal ac
quaintance with the casuals under their
orders is neither necessary nor usual with
the officers over them, and even if It were
none of the offl on the ground
when the mob gathered to set fire to
Rehfeld's whisky barrels. The troopers
who finally restored order had no time to
ascertain just who were the. ones making
the trouble and of tho crowd finally gath
ered In there Is no way of separating the
from th>- goatsâ the good from the
badâ that is. unless some one confesses or
tells tales, and the soldiers will
do neith.r.
The process of arresting suspected sol
diers -without confining them is proceed
ing right along. Two men who had not
been spotted were placed on the list yes
terday and there were more to come, but
the Baloon la dcs til the men know
the game Is in their own hands, so arrests
are not dn
If caught there Is punishment and
enough to spare for those who were guil
ty. They will be turned over to the civil
authorities first and all the years of pris
on the law pn arson will prob
ably become theirs, besides collateral Ken
tences for malicious tnlschi* f, battery, as
sault to murder, threats to kiil and a
dozen other eh â¢ then when the
civil authorities have finished the. mili
tary authorities will form a court-mar
tial to heal the breach in discipline and
a term on Alcatraz will wind the thing
up.
The one man under arrest for the row
on Saturday night is William R. Clark.
He Is believed to be the one who struck
King and knocked him Insensible, and
he has been identified as the assailant by
two soldiers who were In the saloon at
the time. But to the officers who ques
tioned him he declared he knew nothing
of the row and could not imagine why
he had been arrested. King himself knows
nothing of thu row on Saturday night
ar.fl nothing of the subsequent events on
Sunday night. He in now In the hospital
nursing two black eyes, the finest speci
it Is reported, ihat have been ln
trorlu'^d to the institution, but no one is
allowed to see him. Of the others who
were in the fight Earl White is getting
along with the use of one eye, while he
. the other bandaged, and two or
more are rubbing sore spots with
arnica.
The one most injured is Behfeld. the
saloon man. He had $600 insurance on
the place, but he does not know whether
or not hia policy is good under the cir
cumstance of mob violence. So far as
his chanr-M? of securing redress from the
Government are concerned he is not
hopeful. The case will have to bo in
vestigated and reports will be made on
It for the next naif year, but in the
meantime he has a wife and six children
to care for and they can hardly afford to
await the work^igs of the court of
claims. He has no clear idea what he
will do about damages.
The Question of responsibility is a nice
one. The property was beyond the limits
of the reservation, but It was destroyed
by Government employee. The question of
punishment Is an easy one compared to
the one of redress. The civil authority i
la not disputed and the men will nave to 1
answer as criminals from civil life, but
who shall pay for the damage is not so
definite. So far the only offense the mili
tary authorities look upon with concern
ts the i.re.-M'h of discipline; the arson is
looked upon as a civil matter, and the
investigation now being carried on is
more in the nature of an assistance to
the civil authorities than to correct any
military offense committed. The police
will l>e called on to lend what aid they
can give in the case, but that will be but
little. The general opinion is that, un
less the aspect of the matter changes
considerably there will be no result.
Goldberg, the tailor, whose shop con
tributed to the blaze, lias moved his
stock to the Bidewalk across the street
and is already resuming business. Prince,
the barber. Is also looking around for an
other place for himself and his razors,
but Rehfeld is not so sure. He has not
yet decided whn f he will do; whether to
settle again within reach of the soldiers
or to go where soldiers are unknown ex
cept for purposes of war.
The neighborhood has resumed its
usual quiet and but for the burned shells
and the presence of three policemen in
stead of one there is no indication that
the locality is anything but the most
peaceful arid sober. The Fire Marshal
has also taken a look at things, but he,
too, is awaiting the action of the investi
gation by the military authorities. The
officers are -willing to do all in their
power, hut they expect hut little from
any examination of the men. In mobs
where every man is known it has b< en
the experience that no one is ever
caught, and, as one officer expressed it.
what better success can be expected in
this case..
WRIT OF PROHIBITION
SAVES E. C. KILPATRICK
JUDGE DAINGERFTELD GRANTS
TEMPORARY RESPITE,
Enjoins Board of Education From
Deposing the Principal of the
Business Evening School.
The Board of Education ran against a
pnag last night in the shape of an alter
nate writ of prohibition issued by Judge
r>aingerfield. prohibiting it from taking
any action against E. C Kilpatrlck, prin
cipal of the Business Evening School. A
special meeting of the board had been
called for the purpose of trying a number
of teachers charged with incompetency,
among them being Kilpatrick. Thf latter
was also accused of unprofessional con
duct and insubordination, and there is
little doubt but for the Issuance of
the writ he would have been summarily
deprived of his position. As it was, the
board was obliged to postpone action in
his case, and the matter went over until
next Monday night.
Judge Daingerfleld's action in issuing
the writ was based on the contention that
Kilpatrick having once been adjudged
guiltless of the charges preferred against
him he could not again be placed in jeop
ardy on identical charges. The writ is
returnable on April liv
Kilpatrick's troubles date from August,
isr<6, when charges similar to those men
tioned were preferred against him by Ger
aldine. G. Donovan and Walter S. Bran,
teachers In the Business Evening School.
Kilpatrick claimed that the charges wero
instigated by Directors Kagan and Galla
gher, and on recommendation of the
Rules Committee, to which they were re
ferred, they were dismissed. In Febru
ary, 1198, charges were again preferred
against the obnoxious principal by Ral
eigh P. Hooe, and the subsequent trial re
sulted in Kilpatrick being dismissed from
the department on March 23, 1&& S. He car
ried the case to the Superior Court, al
leging that he had not been accorded a
fair and impartial trial by reason of prej
udice on the part of a majority of the Di
rectors, who he claimed were in a con
spiracy to oust him from his position.
Judge Hunt on September 'it, lSi+S, de
cided in his favor and ordered him re
instated. The old board appealed from
this decision, but when the new board
came Into office the appeal was dismissed
and Kilpatrick was reinstated on Febru
ary 10 of this year, i-'resh charges were
almost immediately brought, however,
and these were supplemented on March 8
by charges preferred by Walter S. Bran,
and It was this batch of charges which
the board intended to take up last night.
The writ of prohibition took the board
completely by surprise, but the Directors
are confident that they will be sustained
when the case comes up for argument.
Kilpatrick is marked for decapitation,
and while he may put off the fatal day
by resorting to technicalities, his fate is
sealed.
Superintendent Webster announced that
of the other teachers who were charged
with ineompetency, some had resigned,
others had retired and others were un
able to be present on account of illness,
leaving but one teacher ready to stand
trial, Mrs. Emma Pool, a teacher in the
Haight primary, who desired that her
case be considered behind closed doors.
The request was granted and the board j
â went into committee of the whole, with i
Vice President Head in the chair. When
the board reconvened it was announced
that Mrs. Pool had been dismissed for
physical ineompetency with the under
standing that if within six months her
disability had been removed a petition
for reinstatement would be favorably
considered by the board.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, TUESDAY, APRIL 18, 1899.
KENVILLE IS
ALLOWED HIS
BACK WARRANTS
Paid for Services He
Did Not Perform.
INJURED LITTLEWILLIE LYNCH
CHIEF LEES FAVORS THE SUS
PENDED OFFICER.
Collects Five Months' Salary, Al
though He Had Not Done
Any Duty for That
Time.
Police-man Frank Kenville enjoys the
peculiar distinction of having collected
$500â his salary for five monthsâ from the
city without having performed any police
duty for that period.
Kenville is the Individual who struck
little Willie Lynch with his club during a
prize fight at Woodward's Pavilion. The
unfortunate boy was standing near the
; entrance to the pavilion when Kenvllle,
! evidently anxious to make a record, threw
I his, club at him, striking him In the head
i and fracturing his skull. Although Ken
vllle emphatically denied that he had
thrown his club at his helpless victim,
there were several witnesses to the as
sault who positively identified him as the
officer who had wielded the locust. Satis
fled that Kenville was responsible for the
Injury to the boy, Captain Qillin, to whose
station he is attached, formally charged
him with an assault to commit murder
: and Kenville was at once suspended by
i Chief I,ees pending: the result of his trial
lin the Superior Court. That was tive
' months ago, and although Kenville has
not done any police duty during that
, period he has received the warrants for
his salary, which he did not earn, and
they were duly honored by the Treasurer.
The day after Kenville a trial in the
Superior Court, and which resulted in a
disagreement of the jury, Kenvllle was
I restorer! to duty by Chief Lees. He was
given his hack warrants and told to re
port to ( !aptain < Jillln.
The head of the department explains
the action by saying that notwlthstand
| ing Kenville had not performed po
! lice duty from the time or his suspension
until he was reinstated, he was still a
member of the police force and entitled
to draw his salary for the time that he
was relieved of his star.
Kenville hopes, with the aid of his
"pull." to induce the District Attorney
to withdraw the charge against him and
! thus avoid being tried again.
His unfortunate victim lias not entirely
recovered from the effects of the blow
at the hands of the infuriated policeman,
and it Is doubtful If he ever will. At
certain times, particularly when he gels
exicted, he swoons away. His pitiable
condition, so the surgeons who have in
terested themselves in his case say, is
due to the blow on the head.
WANT THE FOREMAN REMOVED
Shipwrights and Shipcalkers Refuse
to Labor for Boole & Son Until
Daniel Barnes Resigns.
Messrs. William A. Boole & Son, ship
wrights, have fallen into disfavor with
the Shipwrights' and Shipcalkers' asso
clations, owing to the firm's determina
tion to ignore the expressed wish of the
organizations, In that they ask for the
resignation of their foreman, Daniel
Barnes. The latter has been suspended
by the Shipwrights' Association for two
years from acting as foreman for conduct
oming ;i member of the union.
The members of both associations were
to have begun work on the barkentine
Newsboy this morning, but as Boole &
Son decline to abide by the decision of the
union the men will not go to work. Know
ing this. Boole & Son have advertised for
non-union men to take the place of the
It is expected that the firm will have
seen its mistake in fighting the unions
and to-day ask for the resignation of
Foreman Barnes. If it does not reach
such a conclusion the barkentine will be
delayed, as no union shipwright or ship
calk.-r will labor on her until Barnes is
removed.

The Democrats of the Fortieth District

MISS BURLING TO WED
THE OLYMPIA'S DOCTOR
THE swell social set experienced a
pleasant sensation yesterday
when it learned of the engage
ment of Miss Mamie Hurling and
Dr. John Evelyn Page of the Olym
pia.
The betrothal was supposed to be a
profound secret, the charming fiancee
having taken only a very few of her
very dear friends into her confidence,
but the Becret was too good to keep,
and the "little bird" who always
whispers of heart affaJrs noised the
interesting news abroad.
Miss Burling is the daughter of the
late William Burling and for a num
ber of years has beep one of the lead-
Ing belles In select circles. Hit many
charming qualities, together with her
magnetic personality, have endeared
to her hosts of friends. Miss Burling
comes of a family whose name is
closely identitied with the early com
mercial and social history of the
State. In early days William Burling
was a millionaire stockbroker whose
name was a power on the street.
During the past three years Miss
NO OPPOSITION
TO THE WIRES
UNDERGROUND
Only a Question of
What District.
COMPANIES WILL CO-OPERATE
THEY CONFER WITH THE MER
CHANTS' ASSOCIATION.
A Week Hence the District and the
Time Within Which Work Must
Be Done Will Be
Fixed.
The directors of the Merchants' Associa
tion met yesterday with representatives of
the various lighting companies to discuss
the question as to what district electric
wires shall be placed under ground. This
is one of the matters affecting the wel
fare of the public which the association
has agreed to investigate and report-up
on to the Supervisors.
There was a unanimity of opinion at
the conference that the wires should
properly be placed under ground. The
representatives of the various companies
readily agreed to this. The looks of the
city's streets would be greatly improved
with the removal of unsightly poles and
the network of wires overhead. Chief
Sullivan is particularly anxious to do
away with the latter, as they always in
terfere with the proper working of the
Fire Department.
the discussion, which was participated
in by. men who have made the lighting
question a special study, hinged on two
pointsâ the district in which the Officers
of the city, shall order all wires under
ground and the time when the work shall
be completed. The representatives of the
various companies professed their eager
ness to have the wires under ground at
the earliest possible moment. The dis
trict in which this shall be done has not
as yet been defined. This is the only
point upon which there is any difference
uf opinion. Some believe the lines defin
ing tho present fire limits should be
adopted. Others hold this to be too wide,
embracing all the city north of Market
street and east of Stockton to the water
front and a strip two blocks In width
south of Market street from the bay to
Twelfth street.
This will be greatly modified. One of
the plans presented contemplates fixing a
district along certain geographical lines,
while the other plan embraces those
streets of the city that are most trav
eled. Those in attendance at the con
ference could not decide upon the exact
district to be covered and the length of
time in which the work should be don*.
It was determined to meet again on MorP"
day next, at which time the question
will be finally passed upon.
There were present at the conference
representatives of the Sunset Telephone
and Telegraph Company, Pacific Postal
Cable-Telegraph Company, Mutual Elec
tric Light Company and San Francisco
Gas and Electric Company, Superintend
ent Hewitt of the Fire Alarm and Police
Telegraph Department, and Chief Sulli
van of the Fire Department, as well as
President Dohrmann, Director Hale, Sec
retary Freud and Superintendent King,
of the Merchants' Association, were also
, present.
Burling and her mother have made
their home in Southern California,
iind it is only within a few
months that they have once again
taken up their home In this city. Dr.
Page for three years has been
stationed In Asiatic waters. Dur
ing the recent war he was In .the
thick of the fight and was side by sid<;
with Dewey when the Spanish squad
ron mot defeat in Manila Bay.
The engagement of Miss Burling
and Dr. Page is a matter of quite long
standing. For seven years Dr.
Page waited and served faithfully
for the little word that was to make
him the happiest of men. The doctor
comes from an old Virginia family
and his sisters are among the most
beautiful of the many handsome
women lor which Virginia is famous.
I>r. J'age has been away for the past
three years but Is expected here
about the fith of May. No definite
arrangements for the wedding have
been made, but it is known that it
will take place shortly after the doc
tors .irrlval and will be a very quiet
affair.
RAGTIME AND
COMEDY SKETCH
ORPHEUM HITS
Clarice Vance in Coon
town Character.
ART OF LILLIAN BURKHART
THE RAYS OFFER THREE HOURS
OF WIHD FUN.
By Ashtnn Stevens.
The prophets were busy last season
promising us the death of the coon
song. All the "warm babies" and
"chocolate ladies" and the "hottest
coons in Dixie" were to pay the price
of over popularity with their lives. But
for all these gloomy forecasts the coon
sons still lives its reckless rag-time
life, lives it harder than ever. No
vaudeville show, no farce comedy is
complete without it. The adventures
of the colored tenderloin, told in synco
pation, have taken, a strong hold on the
people. Rag-time is more than a fad,
it is a fact. In its sportive pumpkin
colored way it has made an original
contribution to the folksong of Amer
ica. The text of most of these songs is
more or less "tough." Sung in the
white man's dialect it would be posi
tively objectionable in most cases; but
when couched in the flash nigger's
lingo even the frankest of the songs
seem to make a humorous and harmless
appeal to all sorts of people. The
secret of the charm of the modern
coon song is character. There is em
phatic character not only in the music,
but in the stories to which it is set.
I heard a strong specimen at the
Orpheum Sunday night when the new
bill was put on. It was sung by Clarice
Vano, a tall, angular, expressively vis
aged young woman with a voice just
made for rag-timeâ hard, loud and
faultless in articulation. The song is
called "Gimme Back Dem Clothes," and
tells of a dusky wench who won at
"craps" or "policy" and gave her man
the finest in the landâ everything from
silk hosiery to an 18-carat tooth. But
he was Indiscreet and they fell out and
this is the chorus she sings:
Gimme back dat overcoat I buyed yo",
Or.de papers will be full of rag-time
news;
Gimmf back dat fancy checkered vest,
Likewise dem socks and shoes;
Just hand mn over dat solid gold tooth
And take dem eyeglasses off-a yo 1 noseâ
Yo' been coon-rich, but you won't have a
stitch
When yo' hand me back dem clo'es.
Miss Vance sings this song and others
that are not so new with irresistible
emphasis and sideplay. She has the
value of the right word down to a
nicety; and her entire work is some
thing more than mere music hall sing
ingâit Is a sharp, clever study In char
acter.

by Tom and Lily English, which is of

This week, as last, the most artistic
feature of the bill is Lillian Burkhart in
a short comedy. This one is named
"Extravagance." and the programme
candidly states that it is a farcical re
minder of "My Milliner's Bill," a little
piece memorable for its performance by
Roslna Yokes. "Extravagance" is
not notable for any of the delicacies and
fancies that marked Miss Burkhart's
fairy gem of last week; it is farce pure
and obvious, and naturally makes a
heavier dent in the appreciation c-f the
Orpheum-goers. The part of the
thoughtless, extravagant bride of
an embarrassed young stock broker
is worked out with considerable '
consistency and displays a charming I
phase .of Miss Burkhart's comedlc
power. She is the best actress that I
have ever seen in vaudeville. She has
grace, humor, a fine personal spell and
a voice that makes music of every line.
Come to think of it, in our present
plethora of comic opera and farce
comedy, Miss Burkhart at the Orphoum
is the only player in San Francisco
who may be reckoned with seriously. I
wish we might have hours- of her in
stead of minutes.
â¢ * *
When It comes to "A Hot Old Time,"
with Johnnie and Emma Ray in it,
which commenced to burn at the Cali
fornia Theater last night, I throw up
my, hands. When a theaterful of ap
parently normal people laugh to the
shrieking point for three consecutive
acts there must be something funny in
the show. That is what the crowd did
last night. Thin people rattled and
fat people jellied with the force of their
own laughter. The title of the piece
was justified.
The Rays are the same old Johnny
and Emma, the most husky, steamful
team of breakneck vaudevlllalns in the
business. Even the offer of a reward
would not produce the plot in their farce
comedy. It is a whirlwind of horse
play, gag, dance, fight, song and shout.
Mr. Ray's Irishness is more potent than
ever. He does everything that he used
to do in his Orpheum act, and a great
deal besides. The only people in the
world who could make a go of this
show with its supporting company are
the Rays. And they do it.
Notice of the other theater* Is post
poned until to-morrow.
